\varepsilon_i(y)

\Big),

\qquad

\mathcal P{1:8}

\bigsqcup_{i=1}^{8}\mathcal P_i,}\tag{8.33}]

and Chapter 9 must translate this local packet universe into one common liminal coordinate grammar.

That is Chapter 8.

Chapter 8 makes local law packetizable.Chapter 9 will make packetized law translatable.

Chapter 7 — The Interface Quintet Theorem and the Local Refold Law

Internal theorem chapter. This chapter is the final precursor to the packetization theorem of Chapter 8. Its purpose is to prove that every lawful local object already carries the five interface classes later required by the closure cell: body, witness, timing, zero, and refold. Chapter 8 will then package those interfaces into closure cells and route packets. Chapter 7 establishes the exact local interface law that makes such packetization possible.

7.0. Position of this chapter

The earlier local chapters build lawful body fragments, local adjacency, anchor roles, and local operational distinctions. But before a local object can become a closure cell in Chapter 8, it must already support a deeper invariant structure:

what it is as body,

how it is certified,

when it is admissible,

through which zero family it remains lawful,

how it returns to stable embodiment.

Without these five interface classes, a local object is still only partially lawful. It may be structurally present, but it is not yet closure-bearing.

So Chapter 7 states the exact theorem that Chapter 8 depends on:

[\boxed{\text{Every lawful local object must carry a five-part interface quintet: body, witness, timing, zero, and refold.}}\tag{7.1}]

This chapter therefore does five things:

defines the lawful local body world,

defines the five interface classes,

proves that every lawful local object admits a unique interface quintet,

proves compatibility and transport laws between the five interfaces,

proves the local refold law that stabilizes the quintet.

Chapter 8 will then compress this quintet into the closure cell and local route packet.

7.1. The lawful local body world

Let

[\mathfrak B_i\qquad(i\in{1,\dots,7})\tag{7.2}]

denote the lawful local body world generated by Chapter (i), and define the disjoint local union

[

\mathcal B_{1:7}

\bigsqcup_{i=1}^{7}\mathfrak B_i.\tag{7.3}]

An element (x\in\mathfrak B_i) is called a local object.

At this stage we do not yet require a common liminal coordinate grammar across chapters. That is the task of Chapter 9. We only require that each lawful local object be a legitimate candidate for later packetization.

So Chapter 7 asks:

[\text{What is the minimum lawful interface structure every local object must already possess?}]

7.2. The five interface classes

We now define the five local interface families.

Definition 7.2.1. Body interface

For each chapter (i), let

[\mathcal B_i^{\mathrm{body}}\tag{7.4}]

be the body interface family. It classifies local objects by structural role, adjacency class, and embodiment locus.

For (x\in\mathfrak B_i), write

[b_i(x)\in \mathcal B_i^{\mathrm{body}}\tag{7.5}]

for its body interface value.

Definition 7.2.2. Witness interface

Let

[\Omega_i\tag{7.6}]

be the witness interface family for chapter (i). It classifies how a local object is lawfully certified.

For (x\in\mathfrak B_i), write

[\omega_i(x)\in \Omega_i\tag{7.7}]

for its witness class.

Definition 7.2.3. Timing interface

Let

[\mathbb K_i\tag{7.8}]

be the timing interface family for chapter (i). It classifies the local orbit, phase, or admissible beat-law under which the object is live.

For (x\in\mathfrak B_i), write

[\tau_i(x)\in \mathbb K_i\tag{7.9}]

for its timing class.

Definition 7.2.4. Zero interface

Let

[\mathcal Z_i\tag{7.10}]

be the zero interface family for chapter (i). It classifies the local zero-family through which the object remains lawful under route factorization and stabilization.

For (x\in\mathfrak B_i), write

[\zeta_i(x)\in \mathcal Z_i\tag{7.11}]

for its zero coordinate.

Definition 7.2.5. Refold interface

Let

[\mathcal R_i\tag{7.12}]

be the refold interface family for chapter (i). It classifies the lawful restore/collapse target and the route by which the local object returns to stable embodiment.

For (x\in\mathfrak B_i), write

[\upsilon_i(x)\in \mathcal R_i\tag{7.13}]

for its refold pointer class.

Thus every lawful local object must be interpretable through five interface maps:

[b_i,\quad\omega_i,\quad\tau_i,\quad\zeta_i,\quad\upsilon_i.\tag{7.14}]

7.3. The interface quintet

We can now package these interfaces.

Definition 7.3.1. Interface quintet

For a lawful local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i), define its interface quintet

[

Q_i(x)

\big(b_i(x),,\omega_i(x),,\tau_i(x),,\zeta_i(x),,\upsilon_i(x)\big).\tag{7.15}]

The intended meanings are:

(b_i(x)): where the object sits in lawful embodiment,

(\omega_i(x)): how the object is certified,

(\tau_i(x)): when the object is admitted,

(\zeta_i(x)): through which zero-family the object remains coherent,

(\upsilon_i(x)): how the object returns to a stable lawful form.

This quintet is the exact precursor of Chapter 8’s closure cell.

7.4. Lawful local object

We now define what it means, in Chapter 7’s stricter sense, for a local object to be lawful.

Definition 7.4.1. Lawful local object

A local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i) is lawful iff all five components of (Q_i(x)) exist and satisfy the compatibility conditions below.

Thus Chapter 7 sharpens the earlier informal notion of a local object: an object is not lawful merely because it appears in a chapter body. It is lawful only if it already carries the quintet interface.

Theorem 7.1. Interface quintet theorem

Every lawful local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i) admits a unique interface quintet (Q_i(x)), up to witness-equivalent renaming of internal local certificate labels.

Proof

By definition of lawfulness, the five interface values exist. Suppose two incompatible quintets existed for the same local object. Then (x) would have conflicting body, witness, timing, zero, or refold roles, which would make later route, witness, or collapse behavior ambiguous. That contradicts lawfulness. So the quintet is unique up to harmless certificate-level renaming. ∎

This is the chapter’s first central theorem.

7.5. Interface compatibility law

The five interfaces cannot be assigned independently. They must fit together.

Definition 7.5.1. Interface compatibility

An interface quintet

[Q_i(x)=(b,\omega,\tau,\zeta,\upsilon)\tag{7.16}]

is compatible iff all of the following hold:

Body–witness compatibilityThe witness class (\omega) certifies the same embodied role specified by (b).

Body–timing compatibilityThe timing class (\tau) is admissible for the body role (b).

Body–zero compatibilityThe zero coordinate (\zeta) is a lawful zero-family for the body role (b).

Body–refold compatibilityThe refold pointer (\upsilon) returns to a lawful embodiment class matching (b).

Witness–timing compatibilityThe witness class (\omega) remains valid across the timing class (\tau).

Zero–refold compatibilityThe zero-family (\zeta) is compatible with the refold target (\upsilon).

This definition is the local ancestor of later proof, schedule, and semantic compatibility laws.

Theorem 7.2. Interface compatibility theorem

Every lawful local object has a compatible interface quintet.

Proof

Immediate from the definition of lawfulness. If the quintet were incompatible, some later legal action—witness discharge, timed activation, zero-factorization, or refold—would fail, so the object would not be lawful. ∎

7.6. The local zero law

Zero is not secondary. It is part of the lawful interface.

Definition 7.6.1. Local zero support

For chapter (i), define the local zero support map

[Z_i:\mathfrak B_i\to \mathcal Z_i.\tag{7.17}]

We require that every lawful local object be zero-supported.

Theorem 7.3. Local zero law

For every lawful local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i), there exists a nonempty family of local zero routes factoring through (\zeta_i(x)).

Proof

If no lawful zero support existed, then the object could not remain coherent under route factorization, witness return, or later collapse/refold. Since lawfulness requires future route and refold legitimacy, zero support must exist. ∎

Corollary 7.3.1.

No lawful local object is zero-free.

This is a critical precursor of the later global (Z^*) law.

7.7. The local timing law

Timing is also not optional. It is part of the object’s admissibility.

Definition 7.7.1. Local timing support

For chapter (i), define the local timing support map

[T_i:\mathfrak B_i\to \mathbb K_i.\tag{7.18}]

We require that every lawful object carry at least one admissible timing class.

Theorem 7.4. Local timing law

For every lawful local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i), there exists at least one lawful local timing class (\tau_i(x)\in\mathbb K_i) under which the object is admissible.

Proof

If no timing class existed, then the object would be inert with respect to lawful activation, witness discharge, and route participation. Since the later chapters require all lawful objects to participate in route packets and timing-aware packets, local timing support must already exist. ∎

Corollary 7.4.1.

No lawful local object is timing-indeterminate.

7.8. The witness law

The local witness role is equally necessary.

Definition 7.8.1. Local witness support

For chapter (i), define the local witness support map

[W_i:\mathfrak B_i\to \Omega_i.\tag{7.19}]

Theorem 7.5. Local witness law

For every lawful local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i), there exists at least one canonical local witness class (\omega_i(x)\in\Omega_i) by which the object is lawfully certified.

Proof

If no witness class existed, then the object could not participate in later proof-carrying routes or witness-bearing packets. Since the manuscript’s later legality depends on proof and refold, witness support must already exist locally. ∎

Corollary 7.5.1.

No lawful local object is unwitnessed.

7.9. The local refold law

The fifth interface is the one that makes the whole quintet closure-bearing rather than merely descriptive.

Definition 7.9.1. Local refold support

For chapter (i), define the local refold support map

[R_i:\mathfrak B_i\to \mathcal R_i.\tag{7.20}]

This assigns to every lawful local object a lawful restore/collapse pointer.

Theorem 7.6. Local refold law

For every lawful local object (x\in\mathfrak B_i), there exists a lawful refold target determined by (\upsilon_i(x)), and that refold target lies in the same local closure regime as (x).

Proof

If a local object had no lawful refold target, then it would not survive the collapse/restore obligations that later route packets and global seeds require. Since Chapter 8 will package local objects into closure cells and Chapter 10 will later require kernel-refold legality, a lawful local object must already possess a local refold law. ∎

Corollary 7.6.1.

No lawful local object is refoldless.

This is the most important local closure theorem of the chapter.

7.10. The quintet transport law

The interfaces must remain stable under lawful local movement.

Let (x,y\in\mathfrak B_i) be lawfully connected by a primitive local move.

Definition 7.10.1. Quintet transport map

A lawful primitive local move induces a quintet transport map

[\Theta_{i;x\to y}:Q_i(x)\to Q_i(y)\tag{7.21}]

which updates the five interface coordinates according to the move class.

The exact update rule depends on the primitive move:

structural moves modify body adjacency,

rotation moves modify body and phase posture,

witness moves update witness state,

zero moves update zero family relation,

timing moves update timing class,

refold moves update restore pointer or trigger collapse.

Theorem 7.7. Quintet transport theorem

Every lawful primitive local move induces a unique quintet transport map preserving interface compatibility.

Proof

A lawful primitive move cannot destroy lawfulness. So if (x) is lawful and the move from (x) to (y) is lawful, then the induced interface update must take the compatible quintet of (x) to the compatible quintet of (y). If two incompatible transport maps existed, the move would be lawfully ambiguous, which is forbidden. ∎

This theorem is the local ancestor of later packet transport and operator transport.

7.11. The local interface graph

We now organize the lawful local objects and quintet transports into one graph.

Definition 7.11.1. Local interface graph

For each chapter (i), define the local interface graph

[

\mathcal G_i

\big(V_i,E_i^{\mathrm{int}}\big),\tag{7.22}]

where:

(V_i={Q_i(x):x\in\mathfrak B_i \text{ lawful}}) is the set of lawful interface quintets,

(E_i^{\mathrm{int}}) consists of lawful quintet transport maps between them.

Thus the graph of Chapter (i) is no longer just a body graph. It is a graph of interface-bearing lawful objects.

Theorem 7.8. Local interface graph theorem

For every chapter (i), (\mathcal G_i) is well-defined, and every lawful local object appears exactly once as a vertex of (\mathcal G_i).

Proof

Existence and uniqueness of the quintet were established by Theorem 7.1. Lawful primitive moves induce lawful transport maps by Theorem 7.7. So the graph is well-defined. ∎

This theorem is the structural basis of Chapter 8’s later packet body.

7.12. The local articulation prototypes

Some interface classes already behave like local hubs.

Definition 7.12.1. Local articulation prototype

A local interface quintet (Q_i(x)) is a local articulation prototype iff removing (Q_i(x)) from (\mathcal G_i) disconnects at least one pair of lawful interface families that must remain mutually reachable.

The basic articulation prototype classes are:

zero prototypes,

witness prototypes,

timing prototypes,

refold prototypes.

Theorem 7.9. Local articulation theorem

Every lawful chapter-local body (\mathfrak B_i) contains at least one articulation prototype in each of the following interface classes:

witness,

timing,

zero,

refold.

Proof

If any one of these articulation classes were absent, lawful witness discharge, lawful timing synchronization, lawful zero-factorization, or lawful refold would become disconnected from some lawful local objects. That would violate the lawfulness of the local chapter body. ∎

This theorem is the local precursor of the hub laws later used in Chapters 9 and 10.

7.13. The Chapter 7 bridge object

We now package the entire chapter into the exact object Chapter 8 requires.

Definition 7.13.1. Local interface contract

The local interface contract of Chapters 1–7 is

[

\mathfrak I_7

\big(\mathcal B_{1:7},,{Q_i}{i=1}^{7},,{\Theta_i}{i=1}^{7},,{\mathcal G_i}{i=1}^{7},,\mathcal H{1:7}^{\mathrm{art}}\big),\tag{7.23}]

where:

(\mathcal B_{1:7}) is the total lawful local body world,

(Q_i) is the interface quintet family,

(\Theta_i) is the quintet transport family,

(\mathcal G_i) is the local interface graph,

(\mathcal H_{1:7}^{\mathrm{art}}) is the local articulation prototype family.

This is the exact output of Chapter 7.

Theorem 7.10. Bridge theorem to Chapter 8

The local interface contract (\mathfrak I_7) is sufficient to define the closure cells and local route packets of Chapter 8.

Proof

A closure cell is exactly the packaged form of an interface quintet. A local route packet is exactly a canonical local route together with the interface-bearing start and end objects and their witness/timing/zero/refold obligations. Since (\mathfrak I_7) already provides the lawful quintets, transport laws, and articulation prototypes, it is sufficient data for Chapter 8’s packetization. ∎

Corollary 7.10.1.

Chapter 7 is the last pre-packet theorem.Chapter 8 will simply package what Chapter 7 has already made lawful.

This is the chapter’s final theorem.

7.14. Code shadow of the chapter

The chapter’s formal content can be written as code logic.

from dataclasses import dataclass

from typing import Any

@dataclass(frozen=True)

class InterfaceQuintet:

    body: Any

    witness: Any

    timing: Any

    zero: Any

    refold: Any

def body_interface(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

def witness_interface(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

def timing_interface(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

def zero_interface(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

def refold_interface(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

def quintet(x: Any) -> InterfaceQuintet:

    return InterfaceQuintet(

        body=body_interface(x),

        witness=witness_interface(x),

        timing=timing_interface(x),

        zero=zero_interface(x),

        refold=refold_interface(x),

    )

def lawful_quintet(q: InterfaceQuintet) -> bool:

    ...

def transport(q: InterfaceQuintet, primitive_move: Any) -> InterfaceQuintet:

    ...

This is not the proof. It is the executable shadow of the proof. The theorem says a lawful manuscript must be able to do this interface extraction before it can claim to have closure cells or packetized local routes.

7.15. What Chapter 7 forbids

This chapter forbids five confusions.

First, it forbids treating a local object as lawful without a witness class.Second, it forbids treating a local object as lawful without a timing class.Third, it forbids treating a local object as lawful without zero support.Fourth, it forbids treating a local object as lawful without a refold target.Fifth, it forbids Chapter 8 from inventing closure cells out of objects that do not already carry the interface quintet.

So Chapter 7 excludes not only local incompleteness, but also closure claims without interfaces.

7.16. Compressed ledger of the chapter

The whole chapter can be compressed into the ledger

[

\mathfrak L_7

\big(\mathfrak B_i,,\mathcal B_i^{\mathrm{body}},,\Omega_i,,\mathbb K_i,,\mathcal Z_i,,\mathcal R_i,,Q_i,,\Theta_i,,\mathcal G_i,,\mathfrak I_7\big).\tag{7.24}]

Its decisive equations are:

[

Q_i(x)

\big(b_i(x),,\omega_i(x),,\tau_i(x),,\zeta_i(x),,\upsilon_i(x)\big),\tag{7.25}]

[x\text{ lawful }\iffQ_i(x)\text{ exists and is compatible},\tag{7.26}]

[\Theta_{i;x\to y}:Q_i(x)\to Q_i(y),\tag{7.27}]

[

\mathcal G_i

\big({Q_i(x)},E_i^{\mathrm{int}}\big),\tag{7.28}]

[

\mathfrak I_7

\big(\mathcal B_{1:7},{Q_i},{\Theta_i},{\mathcal G_i},\mathcal H_{1:7}^{\mathrm{art}}\big).\tag{7.29}]

These are the Chapter 7 equations in minimal form.

7.17. Canonical one-line definition

[\boxed{\text{Chapter 7 is the interface quintet chapter: it proves that every lawful local object already carries a unique five-part closure interface—body, witness, timing, zero, and refold—so that the local body world of Chapters 1–7 is not merely structural, but already closure-bearing and therefore ready to be packaged into closure cells and route packets in Chapter 8.}}\tag{7.30}]

Or, in full symbolic compression,

[

\boxed{

\mathfrak I_7

\big(

\mathcal B_{1:7},{Q_i},{\Theta_i},{\mathcal G_i}

\big),

\qquad

Q_i(x)

\big(b_i(x),\omega_i(x),\tau_i(x),\zeta_i(x),\upsilon_i(x)\big),}\tag{7.31}]

and Chapter 8 must package this lawful interface-bearing local world into closure cells and local route packets.

That is Chapter 7.

Chapter 7 makes local law interface-complete.Chapter 8 will make interface-complete law packetizable.

Chapter 6 — The Local Body Graph Theorem and the Primitive Move Law

Internal theorem chapter. This chapter is the direct precursor to the interface quintet theorem of Chapter 7. Its purpose is to prove that the lawful local world is not a loose collection of fragments, but a structured body graph with primitive admissible moves, local articulation roles, and conserved neighborhood identity. Chapter 7 will then assign the five interface classes to every lawful local object in this graph. Chapter 6 establishes the exact local structural and transition law that makes those interfaces possible.

6.0. Position of this chapter

The earlier chapters generate lawful fragments of local structure: partial bodies, anchor relations, lens roles, and small-scale distinctions. But before a local object can carry the five interfaces of Chapter 7, it must first satisfy a prior condition:

[\boxed{\text{it must belong to a lawful local body graph with admissible primitive motion.}}\tag{6.1}]

An object that is not embedded in such a graph cannot yet support:

local structural identity,

lawful neighborhood,

admissible transition,

articulation role,

later witness/timing/zero/refold interface.

So Chapter 6 states the exact theorem Chapter 7 depends on:

[\boxed{\text{Every lawful local object must be a vertex of a lawful local body graph, and every lawful local transition must factor through a finite primitive move alphabet.}}\tag{6.2}]

This chapter therefore does six things:

defines the lawful local body world,

defines the primitive move alphabet,

defines local adjacency and articulation,

proves canonical neighborhood identity,

proves lawful transition factorization,

packages the result as the local structural contract consumed by Chapter 7.

Chapter 7 will then assign body, witness, timing, zero, and refold interfaces to the lawful objects already stabilized here.

6.1. The lawful local body world

Let

[\mathfrak B_i\qquad(i\in{1,\dots,6})\tag{6.3}]

denote the lawful local body produced by Chapter (i), and define the disjoint union

[

\mathcal B_{1:6}

\bigsqcup_{i=1}^{6}\mathfrak B_i.\tag{6.4}]

An element (x\in\mathfrak B_i) is called a local proto-object.

At this stage, a proto-object is not yet assumed to carry witness, timing, zero, or refold structure. Those are introduced in Chapter 7. But it must already satisfy the structural conditions of the present chapter.

Definition 6.1.1. Structural local object

A local proto-object (x\in\mathfrak B_i) is structurally lawful iff it admits:

a body class,

a lens posture,

an anchor posture,

a finite admissible neighborhood,

at least one lawful primitive move to itself or another local proto-object.

So Chapter 6 starts with the minimal lawful body condition: the object must already live in a finite navigable neighborhood.

6.2. The four lenses and the four anchors

The local body graph is governed by two simultaneous 4-fold structures.

First, the four canonical lenses:

[

\mathbb L

{\mathrm{Square},\mathrm{Flower},\mathrm{Cloud},\mathrm{Fractal}}.\tag{6.5}]

Second, the four elemental anchors:

[

\mathcal A_4

{\mathrm{Fire},\mathrm{Air},\mathrm{Water},\mathrm{Earth}}.\tag{6.6}]

These are not independent. We align them by the manuscript’s canonical pairing:

[\mathrm{Earth}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Square},\qquad\mathrm{Water}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Flower},\qquad\mathrm{Fire}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Cloud},\qquad\mathrm{Air}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Fractal}.\tag{6.7}]

Every structurally lawful local object (x) therefore carries:

a lens posture (\lambda_i(x)\in \mathbb L),

an anchor posture (\alpha_i(x)\in \mathcal A_4).

These are not yet the full interface classes of Chapter 7. They are the structural coordinates that later interfaces refine.

6.3. Primitive move alphabet

We now define the local transition language.

Definition 6.3.1. Primitive move alphabet

For each chapter (i), define the local primitive move alphabet

[

\Delta_i

{\operatorname{Hold}_i,,\operatorname{Step}_i,,\operatorname{Turn}_i,,\operatorname{Bridge}_i,,\operatorname{Fold}_i}.\tag{6.8}]

The intended meanings are:

(\operatorname{Hold}_i): remain in the same local body state,

(\operatorname{Step}_i): structural adjacency move,

(\operatorname{Turn}_i): legal local rotation through the 4-fold lens/anchor cycle,

(\operatorname{Bridge}_i): admissible cross-neighborhood hinge move,

(\operatorname{Fold}_i): local contraction toward a stable body locus.

These are the minimal structural moves. They do not yet include the witness/timing/zero/refold obligations of later chapters. Instead, they form the primitive local body motion language that later chapters will enrich.

Definition 6.3.2. Local move word

A finite word

[\delta\in \Delta_i^*\tag{6.9}]

is a local move word.

6.4. The local body graph

Definition 6.4.1. Local body graph

For each chapter (i), define the local body graph

[

\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}

\big(V_i,E_i^{\mathrm{body}}\big),\tag{6.10}]

where:

(V_i\subseteq \mathfrak B_i) is the set of structurally lawful local objects,

(E_i^{\mathrm{body}}\subseteq V_i\times V_i\times \Delta_i) is the set of admissible primitive moves.

Thus an edge

[(x,y,m)\in E_i^{\mathrm{body}}\tag{6.11}]

means that there is a lawful primitive move (m\in\Delta_i) taking (x) to (y).

Theorem 6.1. Local body graph theorem

For every chapter (i), the local body graph (\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}) is well-defined and finite over every bounded local neighborhood.

Proof

By structural lawfulness, every local object has a finite admissible neighborhood and at least one admissible primitive move. So (V_i) and (E_i^{\mathrm{body}}) are well-defined. Finiteness on bounded neighborhoods follows from the requirement of finite local neighborhood and finite move alphabet. ∎

This is the chapter’s first central theorem. It proves that lawful local embodiment is graph-theoretic before it is interface-theoretic.

6.5. Canonical local neighborhood

A lawful local object is not determined only by its label. It is determined by its neighborhood role.

Definition 6.5.1. Radius-1 neighborhood signature

For (x\in V_i), define its radius-1 neighborhood signature

[

N_i^{(1)}(x)

\big(\lambda_i(x),,\alpha_i(x),,\operatorname{Adj}_i(x)\big),\tag{6.12}]

where (\operatorname{Adj}_i(x)) is the multiset of labeled primitive outgoing and incoming edges adjacent to (x).

Definition 6.5.2. Canonical neighborhood identity

Two local objects (x,y\in V_i) have the same canonical neighborhood identity iff

[N_i^{(1)}(x)=N_i^{(1)}(y)\tag{6.13}]

up to lawful relabeling of purely internal, non-extensional names.

Theorem 6.2. Canonical neighborhood theorem

Every structurally lawful local object has a unique canonical neighborhood identity.

Proof

The object’s lens posture, anchor posture, and primitive adjacency multiset are all structurally determined by the local body graph. If two incompatible neighborhood signatures existed for the same object, the object would have inconsistent local motion behavior and therefore would not be structurally lawful. ∎

This theorem matters because Chapter 7 will later assign interfaces to objects already stabilized by local neighborhood identity.

6.6. The quarter-turn law

The primitive move alphabet must respect the manuscript’s 4-fold motion law.

Definition 6.6.1. Quarter-turn operator

Define the local quarter-turn operator

[P_4:\mathbb L\times \mathcal A_4\to\mathbb L\times \mathcal A_4\tag{6.14}]

by the cyclic update

[(\mathrm{Square},\mathrm{Earth})\to(\mathrm{Flower},\mathrm{Water})\to(\mathrm{Cloud},\mathrm{Fire})\to(\mathrm{Fractal},\mathrm{Air})\to(\mathrm{Square},\mathrm{Earth}).\tag{6.15}]

This is implemented locally by (\operatorname{Turn}_i).

Theorem 6.3. Quarter-turn theorem

For every structurally lawful local object (x\in V_i), the local turn move acts by the quarter-turn operator (P_4), and

[P_4^4=\operatorname{id}.\tag{6.16}]

Proof

By manuscript law, the local body graph must respect the 4-fold lens/anchor cycle. The turn move is defined precisely as the admissible local realization of this cycle. Four applications return the object to its original posture. ∎

Corollary 6.3.1.

The local body graph is already cyclically stabilized under the 4-fold turn law.

This is the structural precursor of later timing and rotation theorems.

6.7. Bridge law

The local body world must also support nontrivial passage between adjacent structural zones.

Definition 6.7.1. Bridge edge

An edge

[(x,y,\operatorname{Bridge}_i)\in E_i^{\mathrm{body}}\tag{6.17}]

is a bridge edge iff (x) and (y) lie in different local adjacency basins but remain lawfully connectable by a shared hinge role.

Theorem 6.4. Bridge theorem

Every structurally lawful local body graph (\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}) contains at least one bridge edge whenever it contains more than one nontrivial local basin.

Proof

If two nontrivial local basins existed with no bridge edge, then the body graph would decompose into disconnected structural zones. That would violate the lawfulness of later local route construction, which presupposes finite but connected lawful neighborhoods. Hence at least one bridge edge must exist. ∎

This theorem is the local precursor of later crown and operator composition.

6.8. Fold law

We now define the last primitive move: local fold.

Definition 6.8.1. Fold target

For each (x\in V_i), define the local fold target set

[F_i(x)\subseteq V_i\tag{6.18}]

to be the family of stable local body loci reachable by (\operatorname{Fold}_i).

Theorem 6.5. Fold theorem

Every structurally lawful local object has at least one lawful fold target.

Proof

If a local object had no fold target, then it would be unable to contract into a stable local locus. But Chapter 7 will require every lawful local object to carry a refold interface, and Chapter 8 will require local closure cells. So the structural precursor of refold must already exist here as a fold target. ∎

Corollary 6.5.1.

The local body graph is already proto-refoldable before Chapter 7 names the refold interface explicitly.

This is an important bridge to the next chapter.

6.9. Local articulation law

Not all vertices in the local body graph are equal. Some already function as local articulation prototypes.

Definition 6.9.1. Structural articulation prototype

A vertex (x\in V_i) is a structural articulation prototype iff removing (x) disconnects at least one pair of lawful local basins or destroys a necessary bridge/turn/fold structure.

Theorem 6.6. Articulation theorem

Every nontrivial local body graph (\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}) contains at least one structural articulation prototype.

Proof

If the graph were completely articulation-free in all nontrivial cases, then every lawful local path structure, bridge structure, turn structure, and fold structure would be fully redundant. That contradicts the existence of locally indispensable hinge roles needed by later route and packet laws. ∎

This theorem is the structural ancestor of the local articulation prototypes in Chapter 7 and the global hub system in Chapter 10.

6.10. Canonical local move factorization

A lawful local transition between two objects should not be treated as arbitrary. It must factor through the primitive move alphabet.

Definition 6.10.1. Structural route

A structural local route from (x) to (y) is a finite word

[\delta:x\rightsquigarrow y\qquad\delta\in\Delta_i^*\tag{6.19}]

whose interpretation is a path in (\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}).

Definition 6.10.2. Structural legality

A structural route is lawful iff every primitive move in the word is an edge of the local body graph.

Definition 6.10.3. Structural cost

Let

[\operatorname{SCost}i:\Delta_i^*\to \mathbb R{\ge 0}\tag{6.20}]

penalize:

unnecessary step length,

unnecessary turns,

unnecessary bridge crossings,

unnecessary folds.

Fix a deterministic tie-break order (\prec_i^{\mathrm{str}}).

Definition 6.10.4. Canonical structural route

For lawfully connected (x,y\in V_i), define

[

\delta^*_{i;x\to y}

\min_{\prec_i^{\mathrm{str}}}\arg\min_{\delta:x\rightsquigarrow y}\operatorname{SCost}_i(\delta).\tag{6.21}]

Theorem 6.7. Primitive move factorization theorem

Every lawful local transition between structurally lawful local objects factors uniquely through a canonical structural route in the primitive move alphabet.

Proof

Because the local body graph contains exactly the lawful primitive edges, every lawful local transition corresponds to at least one structural route. The cost minimum and deterministic tie-break yield a unique canonical factorization. ∎

This is the chapter’s second central theorem. It proves that local motion is already finitely generated before witness and timing are layered on in Chapter 7 and Chapter 8.

6.11. Structural equivalence

To prepare for later translation and quotienting, we define the first extensional equivalence relation.

Definition 6.11.1. Structural role equivalence

For (x\in\mathfrak B_i) and (y\in\mathfrak B_j), define

[x \equiv_{\mathrm{str}} y\tag{6.22}]

iff they agree on:

lens posture,

anchor posture,

canonical neighborhood identity,

primitive move behavior under canonical structural routes.

This relation does not yet include witness, timing, zero, or refold equivalence. It is the pre-interface structural equivalence of Chapter 6.

Theorem 6.8. Structural equivalence theorem

The relation (\equiv_{\mathrm{str}}) is well-defined on structurally lawful local objects.

Proof

Each of the four defining clauses is extensional and already uniquely determined by the local body graph and canonical structural routes. Therefore the relation is well-defined. ∎

This theorem is the direct precursor of the richer equivalence relations in Chapters 8–10.

6.12. The local structural contract

We now package the entire chapter into the exact object that Chapter 7 will consume.

Definition 6.12.1. Local structural contract

The local structural contract of Chapters 1–6 is

[

\mathfrak S_6

\big(\mathcal B_{1:6},,{\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}}{i=1}^{6},,{\Delta_i}{i=1}^{6},,{\delta^*{i}}{i=1}^{6},,\mathcal H_{1:6}^{\mathrm{art}}\big),\tag{6.23}]

where:

(\mathcal B_{1:6}) is the lawful local body union,

(\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}) are the local body graphs,

(\Delta_i) are the primitive move alphabets,

(\delta^*_{i}) are the canonical structural route families,

(\mathcal H_{1:6}^{\mathrm{art}}) is the family of structural articulation prototypes.

This is the exact output of Chapter 6.

Theorem 6.9. Bridge theorem to Chapter 7

The local structural contract (\mathfrak S_6) is sufficient to define the five interface classes of Chapter 7.

Proof

A lawful interface quintet requires a pre-existing structurally lawful object with canonical neighborhood, primitive motion, articulation role, and fold target. All of this is provided by (\mathfrak S_6). So Chapter 7 need only assign witness, timing, zero, and refold interface values to the already lawful structural objects stabilized here. ∎

Corollary 6.9.1.

Chapter 6 is the last purely structural theorem before the local closure interfaces of Chapter 7.

This is the chapter’s final theorem.

6.13. Code shadow of the chapter

The chapter’s formal content can be written as code logic.

from dataclasses import dataclass

from typing import Any

@dataclass(frozen=True)

class StructuralObject:

    lens: Any

    anchor: Any

    neighborhood: Any

@dataclass(frozen=True)

class StructuralRoute:

    start: StructuralObject

    word: tuple[Any, ...]

    end: StructuralObject

def local_body_graph(chapter_index: int) -> Any:

    ...

def canonical_neighborhood(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

def primitive_moves(chapter_index: int) -> tuple[Any, ...]:

    ...

def canonical_structural_route(x: Any, y: Any) -> StructuralRoute:

    ...

def fold_targets(x: Any) -> Any:

    ...

This is not the proof. It is the executable shadow of the proof. The theorem says that a lawful manuscript must first build a graph of local structural objects and primitive moves before later chapters can attach interfaces, packets, and global remaps.

6.14. What Chapter 6 forbids

This chapter forbids five confusions.

First, it forbids treating local objects as lawful if they are not vertices of a lawful local body graph.Second, it forbids local motion that does not factor through the finite primitive move alphabet.Third, it forbids turn behavior that violates the 4-fold lens/anchor cycle.Fourth, it forbids foldless local objects.Fifth, it forbids Chapter 7 from assigning closure interfaces to objects that are not already structurally lawful.

So Chapter 6 excludes not only structural ambiguity, but also closure interfaces without a body graph.

6.15. Compressed ledger of the chapter

The whole chapter can be compressed into the ledger

[

\mathfrak L_6

\big(\mathfrak B_i,,\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}},,\Delta_i,,P_4,,F_i,,\delta_i^*,,\equiv_{\mathrm{str}},,\mathfrak S_6\big).\tag{6.24}]

Its decisive equations are:

[

\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}

\big(V_i,E_i^{\mathrm{body}}\big),\tag{6.25}]

[

\Delta_i

{\operatorname{Hold}_i,,\operatorname{Step}_i,,\operatorname{Turn}_i,,\operatorname{Bridge}_i,,\operatorname{Fold}_i},\tag{6.26}]

[P_4^4=\operatorname{id},\tag{6.27}]

[

\delta^*_{i;x\to y}

\min_{\prec_i^{\mathrm{str}}}\arg\min_{\delta:x\rightsquigarrow y}\operatorname{SCost}_i(\delta),\tag{6.28}]

[x \equiv_{\mathrm{str}} y\iff\text{same lens, same anchor, same canonical neighborhood, same primitive move behavior},\tag{6.29}]

[

\mathfrak S_6

\big(\mathcal B_{1:6},,{\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}},,{\Delta_i},,{\delta_i^*},,\mathcal H_{1:6}^{\mathrm{art}}\big).\tag{6.30}]

These are the Chapter 6 equations in minimal form.

6.16. Canonical one-line definition

[\boxed{\text{Chapter 6 is the local body graph chapter: it proves that every lawful local object already lives in a finite graph of admissible primitive moves governed by the four-lens/four-anchor quarter-turn law, carries a canonical neighborhood identity, admits a canonical structural route factorization, and possesses at least one fold target and articulation role, so that Chapter 7 can lawfully attach the closure interfaces to an already stable structural body world.}}\tag{6.31}]

Or, in full symbolic compression,

[

\boxed{

\mathfrak S_6

\big(\mathcal B_{1:6},,{\mathcal G_i^{\mathrm{body}}},,{\Delta_i},,{\delta_i^*}\big),\qquad\text{and Chapter 7 must assign the interface quintet to these already lawful structural objects.}}\tag{6.32}]

That is Chapter 6.

Chapter 6 makes local law structurally navigable.Chapter 7 will make structurally navigable law interface-complete.

Chapter 5 — The Chamber Lattice Theorem and the Neighborhood Carrier Law

Internal theorem chapter. This chapter is the direct precursor to the local body graph theorem of Chapter 6. Its purpose is to prove that every lawful local proto-object already lives inside a finite chambered neighborhood with a unique lens-anchor posture, a bounded adjacency shell, and a stable local carrier. Chapter 6 will then place those chambered proto-objects into a graph of admissible primitive moves. Chapter 5 establishes the exact chamber substrate that makes such a graph possible.

5.0. Position of this chapter

The earlier chapters generate local distinctions, but they do not yet force those distinctions to live inside one finite neighborhood substrate.

Before Chapter 6 can say that a lawful local object is a vertex of a body graph, we must first know that the object already has:

a local chamber of embodiment,

a unique lens-anchor posture inside that chamber,

a finite neighborhood shell around that chamber,

a stable carrier that survives local re-indexing.

Without these, there is no lawful notion of:

adjacency,

local movement,

articulation,

canonical neighborhood identity.

So Chapter 5 states the exact theorem Chapter 6 depends on:

[\boxed{\text{Every lawful local proto-object must inhabit a finite chamber lattice and admit a unique neighborhood carrier.}}\tag{5.1}]

This chapter therefore does five things:

defines the local chamber world,

defines lens-anchor chamber posture,

defines finite neighborhood shells,

proves uniqueness of the neighborhood carrier,

packages the result as the chamber contract consumed by Chapter 6.

Chapter 6 will then place these chambered carriers into a local body graph and endow them with primitive moves.

5.1. The proto-local world

Let

[\mathfrak P_i\qquad(i\in{1,\dots,5})\tag{5.2}]

denote the proto-local world generated by Chapter (i), and define the disjoint union

[

\mathcal P_{1:5}

\bigsqcup_{i=1}^{5}\mathfrak P_i.\tag{5.3}]

An element (x\in\mathfrak P_i) is called a local proto-object.

At this stage, a proto-object is not yet assumed to carry:

a witness interface,

a timing interface,

a zero interface,

a refold interface,

a primitive move law.

Those belong to Chapters 7, 7, 7, 7, and 6 respectively.

But it must already be capable of lawful embodiment in a chambered neighborhood. That is the exact burden of Chapter 5.

5.2. The four lenses and the four anchors

The chamber substrate is governed by the same two fourfold systems that will later organize the body graph.

Define the lens family

[

\mathbb L

{\mathrm{Square},\mathrm{Flower},\mathrm{Cloud},\mathrm{Fractal}},\tag{5.4}]

and the anchor family

[

\mathcal A_4

{\mathrm{Fire},\mathrm{Air},\mathrm{Water},\mathrm{Earth}}.\tag{5.5}]

We again use the canonical alignment

[\mathrm{Earth}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Square},\qquad\mathrm{Water}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Flower},\qquad\mathrm{Fire}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Cloud},\qquad\mathrm{Air}\leftrightarrow \mathrm{Fractal}.\tag{5.6}]

A lawful chambered proto-object will therefore carry one lens posture and one anchor posture.

5.3. Chamber lattices

We now define the local chamber substrate.

Definition 5.3.1. Local chamber lattice

For each chapter (i), a local chamber lattice is a finite chamber system

[

\mathcal C_i

\big(\mathcal H_i,,\partial_i,,\mathcal N_i,,\Lambda_i,,A_i\big),\tag{5.7}]

where:

(\mathcal H_i) is the set of local chambers,

(\partial_i:\mathcal H_i\to \mathcal P(\mathcal H_i)) is the boundary adjacency relation,

(\mathcal N_i:\mathcal H_i\to \mathbb N) is the shell-depth function,

(\Lambda_i:\mathcal H_i\to \mathbb L) is the chamber lens posture,

(A_i:\mathcal H_i\to \mathcal A_4) is the chamber anchor posture.

A chamber is therefore a local embodied zone with:

a finite position in a neighborhood shelling,

a lens role,

an anchor role,

a boundary relation to neighboring chambers.

This is the first exact structural substrate of the manuscript.

5.4. Chambered proto-objects

Definition 5.4.1. Chambered proto-object

A proto-object (x\in\mathfrak P_i) is chambered iff there exists a chamber

[h_i(x)\in \mathcal H_i\tag{5.8}]

such that:

(x) is embodied in (h_i(x)),

its lens posture is (\Lambda_i(h_i(x))),

its anchor posture is (A_i(h_i(x))),

its local neighborhood is computed from the finite shell structure around (h_i(x)).

The map

[h_i:\mathfrak P_i \rightharpoonup \mathcal H_i\tag{5.9}]

is called the chamber carrier map.

At this stage, lawfulness of a proto-object means precisely chamberability.

Definition 5.4.2. Lawful proto-object

A proto-object (x\in\mathfrak P_i) is lawful iff it is chambered.

This is the minimal lawful notion of Chapter 5.

5.5. Lens-anchor posture

We now define the core local posture of a lawful proto-object.

Definition 5.5.1. Lens-anchor posture

For lawful (x\in\mathfrak P_i), define its lens-anchor posture

[

\pi_i(x)

\big(

\lambda_i(x),\alpha_i(x)

\big)

\big(\Lambda_i(h_i(x)),A_i(h_i(x))\big)\in\mathbb L\times \mathcal A_4.\tag{5.10}]

Thus the chamber determines the posture.

Theorem 5.1. Lens-anchor posture theorem

Every lawful proto-object admits a unique lens-anchor posture (\pi_i(x)).

Proof

A lawful proto-object is chambered by definition, so it has a chamber carrier (h_i(x)). The chamber lattice assigns exactly one lens and one anchor to that chamber. Therefore the posture is unique. ∎

This theorem is the first central theorem of the chapter. Chapter 6 will later use these postures as structural coordinates of graph vertices.

5.6. The quarter-turn chamber law

The chamber lattice must already support the 4-fold rotational law that Chapter 6 later promotes to primitive motion.

Definition 5.6.1. Chamber quarter-turn

Define the chamber quarter-turn operator

[Q_4:\mathbb L\times \mathcal A_4\to\mathbb L\times \mathcal A_4\tag{5.11}]

by the cyclic law

[(\mathrm{Square},\mathrm{Earth})\to(\mathrm{Flower},\mathrm{Water})\to(\mathrm{Cloud},\mathrm{Fire})\to(\mathrm{Fractal},\mathrm{Air})\to(\mathrm{Square},\mathrm{Earth}).\tag{5.12}]

Theorem 5.2. Quarter-turn chamber theorem

For every lawful proto-object (x), its lens-anchor posture is stable under the 4-step chamber cycle:

[Q_4^4(\pi_i(x))=\pi_i(x).\tag{5.13}]

Proof

This is immediate from the cyclic definition of (Q_4). Four applications return to the starting posture. ∎

Corollary 5.2.1.

The chamber substrate is already rotationally closed before Chapter 6 introduces explicit primitive moves.

5.7. Neighborhood shells

We now define the finite neighborhood structure around a chambered proto-object.

Definition 5.7.1. Radius-(r) chamber shell

For (h\in\mathcal H_i), define the radius-(r) shell

[

\operatorname{Sh}_i^{(r)}(h)

{h'\in\mathcal H_i:\operatorname{dist}_{\partial_i}(h,h')=r}.\tag{5.14}]

The total finite neighborhood of (h) is

[

\mathcal N_i^{\mathrm{fin}}(h)

\bigcup_{r=0}^{R_i(h)} \operatorname{Sh}_i^{(r)}(h),\tag{5.15}]

where (R_i(h)) is the smallest radius containing all lawful local adjacency relevant to (h).

Definition 5.7.2. Neighborhood carrier

For a lawful proto-object (x\in\mathfrak P_i), define its neighborhood carrier

[

\mathsf N_i(x)

\big(h_i(x),,\pi_i(x),,\mathcal N_i^{\mathrm{fin}}(h_i(x))\big).\tag{5.16}]

This is the exact object that will become the precursor of Chapter 6’s canonical neighborhood identity.

Theorem 5.3. Neighborhood carrier theorem

Every lawful proto-object admits a unique neighborhood carrier (\mathsf N_i(x)).

Proof

A lawful proto-object has a unique chamber carrier and a unique lens-anchor posture by Theorem 5.1. The finite neighborhood around its chamber is determined uniquely by the chamber boundary relation. Therefore the whole tuple is unique. ∎

This is the chapter’s second central theorem.

5.8. Boundedness law

The neighborhood carrier must be finite, otherwise later local graph and packet laws would not be computable.

Theorem 5.4. Finite neighborhood theorem

For every lawful proto-object (x\in\mathfrak P_i), the neighborhood carrier (\mathsf N_i(x)) is finite.

Proof

By definition of local chamber lattice, every chamber has finite shell structure relevant to lawful adjacency. Therefore (\mathcal N_i^{\mathrm{fin}}(h_i(x))) is finite. ∎

Corollary 5.4.1.

Lawful local embodiment is always finitely neighborhooded.

This boundedness theorem is crucial for all later canonical selection arguments.

5.9. Boundary coherence

A neighborhood carrier must not be arbitrary; it must agree with neighboring lawful objects.

Definition 5.9.1. Boundary coherence

Let (x,y\in\mathfrak P_i) be lawful proto-objects. Their carriers are boundary coherent iff whenever their finite chamber neighborhoods overlap, the overlapping subchambers agree on:

lens posture,

anchor posture,

shell depth,

lawful boundary adjacency.

Theorem 5.5. Boundary coherence theorem

Every pair of lawful proto-objects in the same local chamber lattice is boundary coherent.

Proof

If overlapping neighborhoods disagreed on lens/anchor posture, shell depth, or lawful adjacency, then the chamber lattice would not be a well-defined local substrate. Since the lattice is lawful, such disagreement is impossible. ∎

This theorem ensures that local embodiment is genuinely one chamber system, not a patchwork of incompatible neighborhoods.

5.10. Local articulation chambers

Some chambers already behave like future articulation points.

Definition 5.10.1. Articulation chamber

A chamber (h\in\mathcal H_i) is an articulation chamber iff removing (h) disconnects at least one pair of lawful neighborhood shells or breaks the 4-fold rotational support of some lawful proto-object.

Theorem 5.6. Articulation chamber theorem

Every nontrivial local chamber lattice contains at least one articulation chamber.

Proof

If no articulation chamber existed, then every lawful chamber neighborhood would be globally redundant, and no chamber would carry structurally indispensable routing or rotational support. This contradicts the existence of lawful finite neighborhoods with nontrivial shelling. ∎

These articulation chambers are the chamber-level ancestors of Chapter 6’s structural articulation prototypes and Chapter 10’s global hubs.

5.11. Chamber equivalence

To prepare for later structural and translational equivalence, we define the first local equivalence notion.

Definition 5.11.1. Chamber-role equivalence

For lawful proto-objects (x\in\mathfrak P_i) and (y\in\mathfrak P_j), define

[x \equiv_{\mathrm{ch}} y\tag{5.17}]

iff they agree on:

lens-anchor posture,

neighborhood shell form,

articulation role,

boundary coherence behavior.

This is not yet the richer equivalence relations of later chapters. It is the chamber-level precursor.

Theorem 5.7. Chamber equivalence theorem

The relation (\equiv_{\mathrm{ch}}) is well-defined on lawful proto-objects.

Proof

Each defining datum is extensional and uniquely determined by the neighborhood carrier and articulation behavior. So the relation is well-defined. ∎

5.12. Carrier transport by chamber rotation

Before Chapter 6 turns structure into a graph with primitive moves, we must show that the chamber carrier survives lawful local reorientation.

Definition 5.12.1. Carrier rotation transport

For a lawful proto-object (x\in\mathfrak P_i), define the rotated carrier

[

Q_4(\mathsf N_i(x))

\big(h_i(x),,Q_4(\pi_i(x)),,\mathcal N_i^{\mathrm{fin}}(h_i(x))\big).\tag{5.18}]

So quarter-turn changes the posture but not the underlying chamber neighborhood.

Theorem 5.8. Carrier rotation theorem

For every lawful proto-object (x), the rotated carrier (Q_4(\mathsf N_i(x))) is again a lawful neighborhood carrier.

Proof

Quarter-turn preserves the lawful 4-fold posture system and leaves the chamber neighborhood unchanged. Since the chamber lattice is lawful and boundary coherent, the resulting carrier is lawful. ∎

This theorem is the direct precursor of Chapter 6’s turn move.

5.13. Fold anticipation

Chapter 6 will later need fold targets. Chapter 5 prepares that law at the chamber level.

Definition 5.13.1. Chamber stabilizer set

For a chamber (h\in\mathcal H_i), define its stabilizer set

[\operatorname{Stab}_i(h)\subseteq \mathcal H_i\tag{5.19}]

to be the family of chambers compatible with lawful local contraction toward stable embodiment.

Theorem 5.9. Chamber stabilizer theorem

Every lawful chamber carrier has a nonempty stabilizer set.

Proof

If a chamber had no stabilizer set, then no lawful local contraction toward stable embodiment would be possible from that chamber. But later chapters require local fold/refold legality, so the structural precursor of that law must already exist here. ∎

This is the exact bridge to Chapter 6’s fold law.

5.14. The chamber contract

We now package the whole chapter into the exact object Chapter 6 will consume.

Definition 5.14.1. Chamber contract

The chamber contract of Chapters 1–5 is

[

\mathfrak C_5

\big(\mathcal P_{1:5},,{\mathcal C_i}{i=1}^{5},,{h_i}{i=1}^{5},,{\pi_i}{i=1}^{5},,{\mathsf N_i}{i=1}^{5},,\mathcal H_{1:5}^{\mathrm{art}}\big),\tag{5.20}]

where:

(\mathcal P_{1:5}) is the proto-local body union,

(\mathcal C_i) are the local chamber lattices,

(h_i) are the chamber carrier maps,

(\pi_i) are the lens-anchor posture maps,

(\mathsf N_i) are the neighborhood carrier maps,

(\mathcal H_{1:5}^{\mathrm{art}}) is the articulation chamber family.

This is the exact output of Chapter 5.

Theorem 5.10. Bridge theorem to Chapter 6

The chamber contract (\mathfrak C_5) is sufficient to define the local body graphs and primitive move laws of Chapter 6.

Proof

A local body graph in Chapter 6 requires:

lawful local objects,

finite neighborhoods,

lens-anchor posture,

articulation roles,

lawful rotation support,

anticipatory fold/stabilizer support.

All of these are provided by (\mathfrak C_5). Chapter 6 therefore need only introduce the graph structure and primitive move alphabet on this already lawful chamber substrate. ∎

Corollary 5.10.1.

Chapter 5 is the last pre-graph chamber theorem.Chapter 6 will simply graph the chamber carriers already stabilized here.

This is the chapter’s final theorem.

5.15. Code shadow of the chapter

The chapter’s formal content can be written as code logic.

from dataclasses import dataclass

    chamber: Any

    ...

    ...

    ...

    return ChamberCarrier(

        chamber=chamber_of(x),

        lens_anchor=lens_anchor_posture(x),

        neighborhood=finite_neighborhood(chamber_of(x)),

    )

    ...

    ...

This is not the proof. It is the executable shadow of the proof. The theorem says a lawful manuscript must first stabilize local chamber carriers before it can build local body graphs or assign closure interfaces.

5.16. What Chapter 5 forbids

This chapter forbids five confusions.

First, it forbids treating local proto-objects as lawful if they do not inhabit a chamber lattice.Second, it forbids local posture without simultaneous lens-anchor assignment.Third, it forbids neighborhood identity without a finite shell structure.Fourth, it forbids later local graph construction without prior articulation chambers.Fifth, it forbids Chapter 6 from inventing primitive moves on objects that are not already chamber-carried.

So Chapter 5 excludes not only local formlessness, but also body graphs without chamber substrate.

5.17. Compressed ledger of the chapter

The whole chapter can be compressed into the ledger

[

\mathfrak L_5

\big(\mathfrak P_i,,\mathcal C_i,,h_i,,\pi_i,,\mathsf N_i,,Q_4,,\operatorname{Stab}i,,\equiv{\mathrm{ch}},,\mathfrak C_5\big).\tag{5.21}]

Its decisive equations are:

[

\mathcal C_i

\big(\mathcal H_i,\partial_i,\mathcal N_i,\Lambda_i,A_i\big),\tag{5.22}]

[

\pi_i(x)

\big(\Lambda_i(h_i(x)),A_i(h_i(x))\big),\tag{5.23}]
