Formally,[\tau=\mathrm{AMBIG}\iff|\mathrm{CandidateSet}|\ge 2\land\mathrm{NoCandidateDecisive}=1\land\mathrm{FrameStillLawful}=1.]AMBIG is not a failure of discipline. It is the disciplined refusal to collapse open ambiguity prematurely. Its mandatory emissions are a candidate set and an evidence plan. The candidate set lists the surviving lawful readings; the evidence plan specifies what would distinguish them. The corpus is explicit that ambiguity belongs in a designated corridor and must be preserved rather than averaged away; Appendix D is where that preservation becomes constitutional rather than tactical.

A claim is assigned FAIL when contradiction, integrity breach, or decisive illegality blocks lawful continuation. Formally,[\tau=\mathrm{FAIL}\iff\mathrm{ContradictionDecisive}=1\lor\mathrm{ReplayBreak}=1\lor\mathrm{PolicyBreach}=1\lor\mathrm{RouteIllegal}=1.]FAIL is not the absence of structure. It is the strongest structured output. Its mandatory emissions are FailurePacket, QuarantineReceipt, and RecoveryPlan. In the live corpus, FailurePacket v1 is already tied to constitutional proofs and the fleet/runtime immune layer, and FAIL is explicitly illegal to suppress, silently merge, or promote. Appendix D therefore treats FAIL as a first-class truth state with maximal downstream governance.

The constitutional core of Appendix D is the no-law vector. The corpus already names the five hard constitutional proof lines that keep the runtime from laundering unresolved or illegal states into closure. Appendix D packages them as

[

\mathcal N_D

\big(n_{\mathrm{escalate}},n_{\mathrm{publish}},n_{\mathrm{rehab}},n_{\mathrm{policy}},n_{\mathrm{merge}}\big),]with the following meaning. No-escalate: a packet may not move to a stronger truth state without newly discharged obligations. No-publish: no public-grade surface may be opened without OK closure and replay sufficiency. No-rehab: a FAIL state may not be re-entered as healthy without repair, replay, and recertification. No-policy-drift: truth tags may not silently change meaning across epochs, routes, or governance contexts. No-silent-merge: contradictory claim histories may not be flattened into one canonical packet without an explicit conflict object and replay-visible resolution. These are not stylistic constraints; they are the constitutional guards that make the truth spine trustworthy.

From these guards, Appendix D derives the constitutional proof object. A constitutional proof is the attested record that a packet’s current truth tag is not merely assigned but legally earned. The canonical form is

[

\Pi_D(\Theta)

\big(\mathrm{TruthTag},\mathrm{NoLawVector},\mathrm{DischargedObligations},\mathrm{BlockingObligations},\mathrm{ReplayStatus},\mathrm{EmissionSet},\mathrm{QuarantinePolicy},\mathrm{PublicationPolicy},\omega_{\Pi}\big).]Its role is simple and absolute: it separates a truth-tagged packet from a lawfully truth-tagged packet. Appendix D requires (\Pi_D) for every packet promoted above raw intake. This is consistent with the corpus’s repeated insistence that witness, replay, and route legality are non-bypass, and that constitutional proof is part of executable legitimacy rather than commentary layered on afterward.

Appendix D also has to define how truth states move. Let the lawful transition operator be[\operatorname{Step}_{\mathbb T}:\mathbb T\times \mathcal E \to \mathbb T,]where (\mathcal E) is the evidence/repair/update event family. The permitted upward motions are:[\mathrm{AMBIG}\to\mathrm{NEAR},\qquad\mathrm{NEAR}\to\mathrm{OK},]and only when the emitted evidence plan or upgrade plan has been fulfilled. The permitted downward motions are:[\mathrm{OK}\to\mathrm{NEAR},\qquad\mathrm{NEAR}\to\mathrm{AMBIG},\qquad{\mathrm{OK},\mathrm{NEAR},\mathrm{AMBIG}}\to\mathrm{FAIL},]whenever replay, route, or constitutional law is decisively broken. This makes the appendix the exact locus of demotion discipline. A truth state is not a static label attached once. It is a governed state in a legal automaton.

Because the appendix sits in the interpretation/governance rail, it must also bind truth states to downstream routing. The canonical routing law is:[\mathrm{Addr}(\Theta)\to \mathrm{AppD}\to\begin{cases}\mathrm{AppI}\to\mathrm{AppM}\to\mathrm{AppO}, & \tau=\mathrm{OK},\\mathrm{AppJ}, & \tau=\mathrm{NEAR},\\mathrm{AppL}, & \tau=\mathrm{AMBIG},\\mathrm{AppK}, & \tau=\mathrm{FAIL}.\end{cases}]This routing law reconciles the corpus’s two main pictures of AppD. In the geometric appendix ring, D appears alongside identity/canonicality. In the executor order, D sits in the entry/interpretation corridor before the truth and replay lanes. The correct synthesis is that D is the place where an addressed packet is constitutionally interpreted into one of the truth-lattice branches and therefore sent to the right downstream organ.

The appendix must also govern publication restraint. The live runtime materials repeatedly sharpen the rule that no public-grade closure may occur without replay, witnesses, and constitutional pass. Appendix D therefore states the publication law:[\operatorname{PublishEligible}(\Theta)=1\iff\tau=\mathrm{OK}\land\Pi_D(\Theta)\ \text{is closed}\land\mathrm{ReplayPass}=1.]Every other truth state is publication-ineligible by default, though NEAR and AMBIG may still be visible internally as work objects and FAIL may still be visible inside quarantine and repair corridors. This separation between internal visibility and public-grade closure is essential for a manuscript that wants both honesty and operational liveliness.

The appendix’s final and deepest task is to convert truth from a passive judgment into a lawful metabolism. The corpus has already crossed that threshold: OK, NEAR, AMBIG, and FAIL now auto-emit their next lawful artifacts. Appendix D is where that rule is fixed constitutionally. That is why this appendix belongs after A, B, and C. A gives the seat. B gives the law schema. C gives the finite carrier and movement substrate. D gives the verdict and the constitutional consequences of the verdict. Only after D can later appendices handle replay tables, crown coexistence, operators, valuation, and semantic closure without ambiguity at the truth layer.

In compressed form, the governing law of Appendix D may be written as[\boxed{\text{A claim is lawfully true only when its addressable packet is assigned a truth state by the constitutional spine, all burdens required by that state are made explicit, and the packet emits the exact downstream artifacts demanded by that state under witness, replay, and no-law constraints.}}]This is why Appendix D is indispensable. It is the place where the manuscript stops merely having states and begins having enforceable verdicts.

Appendix D

Appendix E — Circle Gear: Mixed-Radix Clock, Phase Locks, and Dynamic Scheduling

Appendix E is the manuscript’s temporal dynamics engine. Appendix A gives lawful address. Appendix B gives admissibility law. Appendix C gives the finite square-side kernel and route substrate. Appendix E binds those structures to phase, cadence, recurrence, gate timing, and lawful dynamic execution. In the live corpus, AppE is consistently loaded as the Flower-lens temporal hub: it is the destination of scheduler traffic, the clock authority for kernel execution, the mixed-radix or discrete-time phase coordinator, and the route-side source of cadence rules, timeout guards, and replay clock equivalence. In some branches it is named through the Flower lens and dynamic operators; in others it appears explicitly as the Circle Gear or mixed-radix clock. These are not competing identities. They are the same appendix viewed through structure and runtime: Appendix E is the lawful timing-dynamics layer of the crystal.

The primitive object of the appendix is the timed phase state. A timed phase state is not merely a timestamp. It is a law-bearing position in a discrete recurrence machine that binds chapter motion, operator execution, kernel mixing, and replay equivalence into one temporal packet. The canonical form is

[

\Theta

\big(\kappa,,\varphi,,\Pi_{\mathrm{cycle}},,\Pi_{\mathrm{gate}},,\Pi_{\mathrm{drift}},,\Pi_{\mathrm{sync}},,\omega_\Theta\big),]where (\kappa) is the global cycle coordinate, (\varphi) is the local phase vector, (\Pi_{\mathrm{cycle}}) is the cycle family, (\Pi_{\mathrm{gate}}) is the gate admissibility profile, (\Pi_{\mathrm{drift}}) is the drift-correction state, (\Pi_{\mathrm{sync}}) is the current synchronization relation to neighboring clocks, and (\omega_\Theta) is the witness bundle certifying replay-equivalent temporal identity. This formulation is faithful to the corpus’s repeated insistence that timing is never a scalar add-on; it is a structured state with cycle arithmetic, correction kicks, schedule registries, and proof obligations.

The first invariant of Appendix E is discreteness. Time in the lawful crystal is not modeled as an unconstrained continuum. It is modeled as an indexed lattice or mixed-radix cycle machine. The corpus already makes this explicit in two convergent ways. One branch gives the chapter scheduler as a phase clock with cadence rules, timeout guards, and replay clock equivalence. Another branch develops a full discrete-time cosmological operator algebra over (\mathbb Z), with a one-step shift generator, mutually commuting cycle operators, and periodic correction kicks that keep mythic time aligned with physical time. Appendix E therefore states the discretization law:[\boxed{\text{All lawful temporal evolution in the manuscript factors through a discrete time lattice or a finite mixed-radix quotient thereof.}}]At minimum, every lawful clock in the appendix must therefore admit a carrier[\kappa \in \mathbb Z\quad\text{or}\quad\kappa\in \mathbb Z_{n_1}\times\cdots\times\mathbb Z_{n_m},]with explicit projection and reconstruction maps.

The second invariant is mixed-radix factorization. A single global clock is often too coarse to expose the lawful phase structure of the manuscript. Appendix E therefore adopts a mixed-radix decomposition[\kappa\longmapsto(\kappa_1,\dots,\kappa_m),\qquad\kappa_j\in\mathbb Z_{n_j},]with reconstruction by a pinned Chinese-remainder or equivalent mixed-radix map whenever the cycle family is coprime, or by an explicitly witness-bearing projection when it is not. The point of this factorization is not arithmetic ornament. It makes multiple cycle families—chapter orbit, rail rotation, local packet cadence, replay gates, astronomical or ritual cycles, annealing windows—simultaneously visible without collapsing them into one opaque scalar. The corpus directly supports this by describing AppE as the home of mixed-radix clocking and by giving scheduler nodes phase-op maps, cadence rules, and timeout guards whose semantic content depends on multiple interacting periodicities rather than one raw counter.

To make the clock executable, Appendix E introduces the primary shift operator[\hat U:|n\rangle \mapsto |n+1\rangle.]All local cycle operators are derived from this generator by projection or quotient. If a cycle family has period (P), its cycle operator is[\hat U_P = \hat U \bmod P.]The crucial law, already made explicit in the live corpus, is that such cycle operators commute when they are all derived from the same primary shift:[[\hat U_{P_i},\hat U_{P_j}] = 0.]This law is the deep reason Appendix E can coordinate many periodic structures without semantic collision. Different cycle families may carry different meanings, but their temporal projection remains coherent because they live on one underlying lattice.

However, discreteness alone is not enough. Real lawful timing requires phase locks. A phase lock is a relation between two cycle states asserting that their projected phases satisfy an admissibility condition. Let two cycle states be

[

\Theta_a=(\kappa_a,\varphi_a,\dots),

\qquad

\Theta_b=(\kappa_b,\varphi_b,\dots).

]

A phase lock is a predicate

[

\operatorname{Lock}(\Theta_a,\Theta_b)=1

]

whenever the relevant phase difference lies inside the admissible corridor:

[

\Delta\varphi

\varphi_b-\varphi_a

\in

\mathcal W_{\mathrm{lock}}.

]

The appendix therefore defines a gate family

[

\mathcal G_E

{g_{\mathrm{align}},g_{\mathrm{release}},g_{\mathrm{return}},g_{\mathrm{publish}},g_{\mathrm{repair}}},]each a Boolean function over the current mixed-radix state. A route, packet, or runtime action is lawful only if its required gate is open. This directly synthesizes the live scheduler materials, the mixed-radix/cycle documents, and the broader runtime law that no promotion, replay, or release may occur outside its gate window.

A lawful scheduler must therefore be defined. The canonical scheduler object is

[

\mathcal S

\big(

\mathcal Q,,

\Theta_0,,

\mathcal G_E,,

\mathcal D_E,,

\mathcal A_E,,

\omega_{\mathcal S}

\big),

]

where (\mathcal Q) is the scheduled queue of events, (\Theta_0) is the current phase state, (\mathcal G_E) is the gate set, (\mathcal D_E) is the drift-correction engine, (\mathcal A_E) is the annealing or cadence adjustment law, and (\omega_{\mathcal S}) certifies deterministic replay. The scheduler’s basic transition is

[

\mathcal S_{t+1}

\operatorname{Tick}(\mathcal S_t)

\big(\operatorname{Advance}(\Theta_t),,\operatorname{Release}(\mathcal Q_t,\Theta_t),,\operatorname{Correct}(\Theta_t),,\operatorname{Log}(\Theta_t)\big).]The live corpus already treats the scheduler exactly this way in chapter-side runtime traffic: it exports a phase clock, cadence rules, timeout guards, phase-op maps, and replay clock equivalence, all of which belong to one timed transition engine rather than scattered utilities.

Drift correction is the next indispensable law. A finite or integer lattice used to model real recurrence will drift from measured phenomena unless stabilizers are admitted. The astronomical and ritual corpus makes this explicit through correction kicks such as the periodic (-4) day Venus-table adjustment; the runtime corpus makes the same point more abstractly through schedule digests, replay clock equivalence, and annealing schedules. Appendix E therefore defines the correction kick operator[\hat K_\Delta:|n\rangle \mapsto |n+\Delta\rangle,\qquad\Delta\in\mathbb Z,]with the invariants[\hat K_\Delta^{-1}=\hat K_{-\Delta},\qquad[\hat K_\Delta,\hat U]=0.]The operational meaning is clear: drift repair does not invent a new time axis. It repositions the current state on the same lawful lattice. The scheduler’s drift engine (\mathcal D_E) is therefore a finite-state machine that chooses (\Delta) at predeclared checkpoints when accumulated phase error breaches the admissible threshold. This is a direct synthesis of the corpus’s calendrical correction-kick law and the chapter scheduler’s annealing and timeout discipline.

The appendix must also formalize cadence. A cadence law is not just “how fast things happen,” but how successive legal events are spaced relative to one another. Let a schedule queue be[\mathcal Q = (e_1,\dots,e_r),]with each event (e_j) carrying a desired phase class and a timeout bound. The cadence map[\operatorname{Cad}:\mathcal Q\times \Theta \to \mathbb Z_{\ge 0}]assigns the lawful wait interval before the next release. If the current gate profile cannot satisfy the next event, the scheduler either delays the event within bound, emits NEAR with a residual timing ledger, or routes to FAIL if a hard timing law is violated. This law is required because the live corpus repeatedly distinguishes between stable release, annealed delay, and timeout failure, and because replay equivalence must account not only for event order but also for lawful timing deformation.

Appendix E must also connect directly to packet transport. In the route and runtime corpus, packet semantics already depend on scheduler and transport graphs, and route-to-gate paths for timing-sensitive chapters explicitly pass through AppE before returning to AppC or downstream truth infrastructure. That means AppE is not merely “the clock”; it is the temporal part of packet circulation. The canonical transport-timing coupling is

[

\mathrm{PacketMove}

\big(\mathrm{Payload},,\Theta_{\mathrm{release}},,\Theta_{\mathrm{arrival}},,\mathrm{GateWitness},,\mathrm{ClockWitness}\big).]A packet may therefore not be declared transported merely because it changes location. It must also carry the release phase, arrival phase, and the witness that the relevant gate window and replay clock remained lawful. This is why AppE belongs in the route path for scheduler and transport chapters.

Because Appendix E is a Flower-lens appendix in the current outer-crystal routing, it must also carry dynamical rather than purely structural semantics. The correct synthesis is that AppE governs oscillatory, annealing, attractor-adjacent, and release-window dynamics on top of the discrete lattice. Let[\mathcal A_E:\Theta \mapsto \Theta']be the annealing law. Then a lawful annealed state change is one that preserves replay-equivalence class while changing local phase admissibly to reduce drift, improve synchronization, or realize a delayed release. The manuscript must permit this because otherwise all timing pressure would have to resolve in binary accept/reject logic, which the live corpus explicitly rejects. Annealing is therefore a legitimate Flower-lens temporal refinement of the Square-lens schedule kernel.

The appendix must define three classes of timing witnesses. A cycle witness certifies that a state belongs to a declared cycle family. A gate witness certifies that a specific action occurred within the appropriate release or return window. A replay-clock witness certifies that the same event sequence reconstructed under pinned conditions yields the same phase trace. The canonical timing witness pack is

[

\omega_E

\big(\omega_{\mathrm{cycle}},\omega_{\mathrm{gate}},\omega_{\mathrm{replay}}\big).]No lawful scheduler, transport packet, attractor update, or publication gate may execute without the relevant subset of (\omega_E). The runtime corpus’s repeated insistence on schedule digests and replay clock equivalence already forces this result. Appendix E makes it explicit.

The failure modes of the appendix are also exact and must be stated. There are at least six canonical failure families.

First, phase drift overflow, where accumulated error exceeds admissible correction range.Second, illegal gate crossing, where an event fires outside its release window.Third, clock incoherence, where two supposedly phase-locked cycles diverge beyond admissible tolerance.Fourth, nonreplayable timing, where a schedule cannot be reproduced under identical initial conditions.Fifth, cadence starvation, where lawful delay is exhausted before a release becomes admissible.Sixth, unsignaled correction, where a correction kick changes phase without a corresponding witness and ledger entry.

Each of these must be surfaced as a typed temporal contradiction or quarantine event, not folded back into generic error language. This requirement follows from Appendix B’s law discipline and from the live corpus’s repeated treatment of timing failure as an explicit route and replay breach.

The final and deepest role of Appendix E is to make recurrence lawful. Appendix C gives the kernel carrier; Appendix E gives it orbit. Appendix C gives finite moves; Appendix E gives release order, drift repair, and recurrent admissibility. This is why the two appendices are repeatedly paired in the corpus. AppC without AppE is structure without pulse. AppE without AppC is pulse without finite carriage. Together they yield executable rhythm. This is why the Flower lens is appropriate: it is not softness added to structure, but the lawful pattern of recurrence that lets structure become alive.

In compressed form, the governing law of Appendix E may be written as[\boxed{\text{A lawful dynamic event exists if and only if it is realized on a discrete or mixed-radix time lattice, released through an admissible phase lock and gate window, corrected only by witnessed stabilizers, and replayable as the same phase trace under pinned conditions.}}]This is why Appendix E follows Appendix C. Once the manuscript knows where things are and how they may move, it must also know when they may become real.

Appendix E

Appendix F — Corridor Algebra, Truth Typing, and Requiredness Maps

Appendix F is the manuscript’s admissibility engine. Appendix A fixes where a claim, packet, operator, or artifact sits. Appendix B states the law under which it may exist. Appendix D assigns a constitutional truth state and forces downstream consequences. Appendix F stands between these layers and determines the corridor conditions under which any of those verdicts are even legal. In the live corpus, this role is explicit and stable: AppF is the Flower-side corridor algebra, truth typing, and requiredness hub; it normalizes (\mathbb T)-judgments, corridor tiers, requiredness per scope, abstention policy, and admissibility budgets, and it routes every verification plan, every path to (\mathrm{OK}), and every downgrade protocol for (\mathrm{NEAR}) and (\mathrm{AMBIG}).

The appendix therefore has one governing thesis:

[\boxed{\text{No claim, route, transform, or publication may be judged by raw evidence alone; it must first be interpreted through a typed corridor that specifies what is required, what is allowed, what is forbidden, and what degree of truth is admissible under the active budgets.}}]

This is why Appendix F is not reducible to truth labels, metrics, or proofs. It is the layer that determines which labels are legal, which proofs are required, which metrics matter, which abstentions are correct, and which upgrades or downgrades are even admissible. Without it, the manuscript would still have addresses, law schemas, kernels, and truth states, but it would lack the algebra that binds them into one coherent decision regime. The current corridor-first routing logic in the corpus already makes this exact point: every verification plan, every route to (\mathrm{OK}), and every downgrade protocol is forced through AppF before replay, publication, or governance can complete.

The primitive object of Appendix F is the corridor. A corridor is not a vague context. It is a typed admissibility bundle

[

\mathsf{Corr}

\big(\mathsf{CorrID},\mathsf{Tier},\mathsf{Budgets},\mathsf{GateLang},\mathsf{Req},\mathsf{RegistryPins},\mathsf{LedgerPins},\omega_{\mathsf{Corr}}\big),]where (\mathsf{CorrID}) is the stable identity of the corridor, (\mathsf{Tier}) is the corridor class, (\mathsf{Budgets}) is the active vector of drift, complexity, evidence, and transport caps, (\mathsf{GateLang}) is the frozen gate language for admissibility evaluation, (\mathsf{Req}) is the requiredness map by scope, (\mathsf{RegistryPins}) bind the corridor to fixed naming and schema epochs, (\mathsf{LedgerPins}) bind it to specific publication and replay ledgers, and (\omega_{\mathsf{Corr}}) certifies that the corridor itself is lawfully installed. This structure is directly aligned with the live corpus, which already defines corridors as typed records carrying tiers, budgets, gate languages, requiredness, registry pins, and ledger pins rather than treating them as prose-level settings.

The first invariant of the appendix is corridor-first evaluation. For any candidate claim or action (x), no evaluation is lawful until its active corridor is fixed. Thus the canonical order is[\text{address} \to \text{law schema} \to \text{corridor selection} \to \text{admissibility evaluation} \to \text{truth typing} \to \text{proof closure}.]This order is non-negotiable. A metric, witness, or replay result interpreted outside a fixed corridor does not yet count as a lawful manuscript event. That principle is already explicit in the live compute corpus, where corridor identity is part of the deterministic routing substrate, and every higher judgment is indexed by corridor pins before it is allowed to propagate.

The second invariant is typed truth. Appendix F does not invent the truth lattice—that constitutional role belongs to Appendix D—but it defines the admissibility semantics under which the truth lattice may be used. Let[\mathbb T={\mathrm{OK},\mathrm{NEAR},\mathrm{AMBIG},\mathrm{FAIL}}.]Appendix F assigns to a candidate (x) not a single truth state first, but an admissible truth set[\operatorname{Allow}{\mathsf{Corr}}(x)\subseteq \mathbb T.]Only states in (\operatorname{Allow}{\mathsf{Corr}}(x)) may be constitutionally assigned. This distinction is essential. Appendix D may later prove that (x) is (\mathrm{OK}), but if (\mathrm{OK}\notin \operatorname{Allow}_{\mathsf{Corr}}(x)), that promotion is illegal. Thus the corridor layer is prior to constitutional verdict. The live corpus already encodes this structure implicitly by routing all truth-typing, requiredness, admissibility, and downgrade logic through AppF before replay and publication.

The most important normative law of the appendix is the abstention law:[\boxed{\mathrm{ABSTAIN}>\mathrm{GUESS}.}]This means that if a candidate state is underdetermined under the active corridor, the lawful outcome is (\mathrm{AMBIG}), not forced closure. If evidence is bounded but incomplete, the lawful outcome may be (\mathrm{NEAR}), provided residuals and obligations are surfaced. If contradiction or illegality is decisive, the lawful outcome is (\mathrm{FAIL}). The manuscript therefore rejects both optimism bias and rhetorical completion. Appendix F is where that rejection becomes exact, because it is the appendix that decides which truth states are permitted under which information and budget conditions. The current corridor documents and metro descriptions make this principle explicit by naming AppF as the home of abstention policy and typed admissibility.

To make this exact, Appendix F defines a requiredness lattice. Let the scope space be

[

\mathcal S_{\mathrm{scope}}

{\mathrm{ATOM},\mathrm{CELL},\mathrm{FACET},\mathrm{LENS},\mathrm{CHAPTER},\mathrm{APPENDIX},\mathrm{MANUSCRIPT},\mathrm{RUNTIME}}.]For each scope (s\in\mathcal S_{\mathrm{scope}}) and obligation kind (o), define[\operatorname{Req}(s,o)\in{\mathrm{FORBID},\mathrm{MAY},\mathrm{MUST}}.]The exact meaning is as follows. (\mathrm{FORBID}) means the operation or promotion is illegal at that scope. (\mathrm{MAY}) means it is legal but optional. (\mathrm{MUST}) means the operation or evidence burden is mandatory before a stronger state or publication action is allowed. This requiredness map is one of the live corpus’s defining roles for AppF, and it is indispensable because the manuscript is multi-scale. A witness that is optional at cell scope may be mandatory at chapter or publication scope. A replay check that is unnecessary for a speculative internal branch may be compulsory for OK publication. Appendix F is where that scaling discipline is frozen.

The requiredness map induces the basic corridor admissibility predicate. Let (x) be a candidate and (c=\mathsf{Corr}) the active corridor. Then[\operatorname{Adm}_c(x)=1\iff\Big(\operatorname{SchemaOK}(x)\land\operatorname{PinsOK}(x,c)\land\operatorname{ReqClosed}(x,c)\land\operatorname{BudgetsOK}(x,c)\land\operatorname{GatePass}(x,c)\Big).]This predicate has to be evaluated before any stronger truth promotion, replay seal, or publication move. It is also the central bridge from Appendix B to Appendix D: B defines the law schema and obligation families; F computes whether the relevant obligations are actually in force and satisfied for this corridor; D then issues the constitutional truth result. In this sense F is the manuscript’s admissibility interpreter.

The appendix’s budget layer must also be explicit. The live corpus already identifies AppF with corridor tiers and admissibility budgets, and the CUT materials stabilize the core budget vector as

[

\beta

(\beta_{\mathrm{commute}},\beta_{\mathrm{drift}},\beta_{\mathrm{complexity}},\beta_{\mathrm{evidence}}).]Appendix F adopts this vector as canonical. (\beta_{\mathrm{commute}}) bounds allowed noncommutation under transport and dualization. (\beta_{\mathrm{drift}}) bounds admissible deviation between claimed and realized values. (\beta_{\mathrm{complexity}}) bounds verifier, solver, and route costs. (\beta_{\mathrm{evidence}}) fixes minimum support density for stronger truth states. The corridor law is therefore:[\operatorname{BudgetsOK}(x,c)=1\iff\forall j,\ \mu_j(x)\le \beta_j(c),]where (\mu_j(x)) is the measured burden on the (j)-th axis. This is exact enough for mechanization and faithful to the live corpus’s budget-bearing corridor semantics.

Because corridors are dynamic rather than static, Appendix F must define corridor tiers. The manuscript requires at least a partial order on tiers[\mathcal T_{\mathrm{corr}}]with stricter corridors requiring at least as much evidence, replay closure, and budget discipline as weaker corridors. The exact names may vary by manuscript branch, but the order itself is invariant. If (c_1\preceq c_2), then anything lawful in (c_2) is at least as constrained as in (c_1). The natural monotonicity law is:[c_1\preceq c_2\Longrightarrow\operatorname{Allow}{c_2}(x)\subseteq \operatorname{Allow}{c_1}(x).]Thus corridor strengthening can only reduce or preserve admissible truth states; it can never make a previously illegal strong truth state legal without additional evidence or law change. This monotonicity is crucial for Chapters 18–20, where value, revaluation, and reaxiomatization must all preserve earlier admissibility semantics.

The gate language (\mathsf{GateLang}) is the next indispensable layer. A corridor is not just a scalar threshold. It is a typed conjunction-disjunction system over atomic gates. Let the gate family be

[

\mathcal G

{g_1,\dots,g_n},\qquadg_i : X\times \mathsf{Corr}\to {0,1}.]Then (\mathsf{GateLang}) is a frozen DAG over (\mathcal G) describing which gates are evaluated, in what order, under what dependency relations, and how failures or ambiguities propagate. This is one of the strongest live roles of AppF in the current corpus: corridor algebra is not just thresholds but the active gate machinery through which admissibility is tested. Appendix F therefore fixes two canonical laws. First, gate order must be deterministic. Second, gate semantics are part of the corridor identity and may not silently drift between epochs, packs, or publication contexts.

A lawful candidate evaluation therefore yields not only a truth class but a corridor verdict bundle:

[

\mathcal V_F(x,c)

\big(\operatorname{Allow}_c(x),\operatorname{ReqState}_c(x),\operatorname{BudgetState}_c(x),\operatorname{GateTrace}_c(x),\operatorname{DowngradePlan}_c(x),\omega_F\big).]This bundle is the immediate output of Appendix F. If (\mathrm{OK}\in\operatorname{Allow}_c(x)) and all must-obligations are discharged, the packet may proceed toward Appendix L for replay-verifier closure and Appendix D for constitutional sealing. If (\mathrm{NEAR}\in\operatorname{Allow}_c(x)) but (\mathrm{OK}\notin\operatorname{Allow}_c(x)), the packet must emit residuals and bounded obligations. If only (\mathrm{AMBIG}) is legal, the packet must emit candidates and an evidence plan. If only (\mathrm{FAIL}) is legal, the packet must emit a typed failure corridor result. The appendix is therefore the router from possibility to permitted verdict.

This routing role is reinforced by the live transfer lines. AppF routes toward AppL and AppD when the packet needs replay-backed truth certification, toward AppJ and AppK when corridor legality depends on budgets and transport/commutation, and toward AppP/AppD when governance or publication consequences are triggered. These transfers are not arbitrary. They are the exact architecture forced by the corridor algebra: determine admissibility, then either certify, budget-check, transport-check, or govern. Appendix F is the switchyard of that architecture.

Appendix F must also define downgrade law. A candidate may not remain in a stronger truth state once its corridor conditions are broken. Accordingly, define the downgrade operator[\operatorname{Down}_c:\mathbb T\times \mathcal E \to \mathbb T]with the minimum constraints[\operatorname{Down}_c(\mathrm{OK},e)\in{\mathrm{NEAR},\mathrm{AMBIG},\mathrm{FAIL}},][\operatorname{Down}_c(\mathrm{NEAR},e)\in{\mathrm{AMBIG},\mathrm{FAIL}},][\operatorname{Down}_c(\mathrm{AMBIG},e)=\mathrm{FAIL}]whenever contradiction or corridor breach is decisive. Appendix F does not itself adjudicate the constitutional truth spine—that remains Appendix D’s role—but it declares the only downgrade paths legally available under corridor pressure. This is why the live corpus explicitly associates AppF with “every downgrade/NEAR/AMBIG protocol.”

The appendix must further forbid silent coercions. At minimum the following are illegal:

coercing (\mathrm{AMBIG}\to\mathrm{OK}) without new evidence and corridor reevaluation,

hiding requiredness changes behind new route names or scope shifts,

widening budgets without a declared corridor migration,

scoring or optimizing before gate legality is determined,

silently dropping residuals when only NEAR is admissible,

allowing publication when replay closure is still only MAY rather than MUST.

These prohibitions are the operational content of the corridor-first doctrine already present in the current compute corpus and appendix map. They are what keep AppF from degenerating into a loose “confidence appendix.”

Because the manuscript is generator-first, Appendix F must also store its laws compactly. The correct storage object is the corridor schema kernel

[

\mathcal K_F

\big(\mathcal T_{\mathrm{corr}},\mathcal G,\operatorname{Req},\beta,\operatorname{Allow},\operatorname{Down}\big),]from which every specific corridor can be instantiated by registry pins and policy IDs. This is the compressed form of the corridor civilization. The appendix should therefore be written so that a future compiler can derive concrete corridor records from (\mathcal K_F) plus a small configuration bundle, rather than duplicating law prose for every route.

The final and deepest role of Appendix F is to make lawful uncertainty routable. Without F, the manuscript would still know how to prove and replay, but it would not know when to stop, when to defer, when to publish, or when to abstain. Appendix F is therefore where epistemic discipline becomes operational. It turns “be careful” into gate algebra, “insufficient evidence” into typed admissibility, and “this may still become true” into an exact NEAR upgrade protocol. This is why it belongs in the Flower line: it is not pure structure, but the dynamic legality pattern by which the manuscript decides how strongly reality may currently be stated.

In compressed form, the governing law of Appendix F may be written as[\boxed{\text{A manuscript judgment is lawful if and only if its active corridor makes that truth state admissible, all MUST-obligations at the relevant scope are discharged, all governing budgets remain within bound, and every downgrade, abstention, or publication consequence is explicitly routed rather than silently inferred.}}]This is why Appendix F is indispensable. It is the place where possibility becomes permitted judgment.

Appendix F

Appendix G — Fire Arc Hub: Recipe Packets, Dosage Logic, and Tria Prima Control

Appendix G is the manuscript’s fire-side execution hub. Appendix A seats an object, Appendix B states the law that governs it, Appendix C provides the finite kernel on which it can move, Appendix D classifies its truth state, Appendix E times its release, and Appendix F determines what the active corridor permits. Appendix G takes an already addressed, law-bound, carrier-realized, truth-typed, time-gated, corridor-admissible object and turns it into a directed executable recipe. In the live corpus, AppG is repeatedly assigned this exact role: it is the Fire Arc Hub, the home of recipe packet families, dosage, jar macroframes, and the triadic control law that routes constructive ignition, transport, and termination through the Su/Me/Sa rail system. In one branch it appears explicitly as “Triangle Control & Tria Prima Legality”; in another it is the Fire hub paired with recipe chemistry and dosage frames. These are not competing identities. They are the same appendix seen from two sides: the triadic control grammar is the control law of the recipe packet, and the recipe packet is the executable embodiment of that law.

The governing thesis of Appendix G is therefore:

[\boxed{\text{A lawful executable act in the manuscript exists only when it is packaged as a typed recipe packet, allocated through the triadic fire control, bounded by explicit dosage law, and terminated under a proved recursion ceiling.}}]

This appendix is necessary because the manuscript cannot stop at routes, packets, and truth states. It must also say how lawful structure is ignited into action. Without AppG, the system would still know how to name a claim, how to prove it, and how to time it, but it would not yet know how to execute a bounded action sequence that transforms state without falling into uncontrolled recursion, semantic drift, or corridor breach. The live corpus already makes this exact demand by coupling AppG to Chapters 10–15, where manifestation, compilation, jar chemistry, benchmark fire, and export interfaces all require a fire-side operator layer rather than mere route legality.

The primitive object of Appendix G is the recipe packet. A recipe packet is not just an ordered list of steps. It is a typed executable bundle carrying ingredients, control rails, dosage, recursion budget, and witness-bearing phase history. The canonical form is

[

\mathcal R

\big(\mathrm{RecipeID},\mathrm{Intent},\mathrm{Inputs},\mathrm{TriadPlan},\mathrm{Dosage},\mathrm{JarFrame},D_{\mathrm{rec}},\Theta_{\mathrm{gate}},\mathrm{WitnessSet},\mathrm{ResidualPolicy},\mathrm{TerminationPolicy},\omega_{\mathcal R}\big),]where (\mathrm{RecipeID}) is the stable identity of the recipe, (\mathrm{Intent}) is the target action family, (\mathrm{Inputs}) is the typed operand set, (\mathrm{TriadPlan}) is the Su/Me/Sa execution braid, (\mathrm{Dosage}) is the bounded intensity and quantity vector, (\mathrm{JarFrame}) is the macroframe or reaction vessel in which the packet is allowed to unfold, (D_{\mathrm{rec}}) is the recursion depth budget, (\Theta_{\mathrm{gate}}) is the timing gate witness imported from Appendix E, (\mathrm{WitnessSet}) is the proof and audit support required for action legality, (\mathrm{ResidualPolicy}) declares what happens when exact closure is not reached, (\mathrm{TerminationPolicy}) declares when the packet must stop, and (\omega_{\mathcal R}) certifies that the packet itself is lawful. This formulation is the natural synthesis of the live corpus’s AppG role statements: recipe packet families, dosage, jar macroframes, triadic nodes Su/Me/Sa, and recursion depth constraints belong to one object and not to separate appendices.

The first invariant of the appendix is triadic control. Every lawful recipe must be factored through the tria prima control basis[\mathbb T_3={\mathrm{Su},\mathrm{Me},\mathrm{Sa}},]where:

(\mathrm{Su}) is ignition, allocation, catalyst release, or generative push,

(\mathrm{Me}) is routing, mixing, transport, or mediation,

(\mathrm{Sa}) is sealing, termination, grounding, or bound enforcement.

A recipe packet therefore carries a control braid

[

\mathrm{TriadPlan}

\big(t_1,\dots,t_n\big),\qquadt_k\in\mathbb T_3.]The manuscript’s executive law is that no nontrivial recipe may collapse entirely into one triadic phase. If a packet is pure (\mathrm{Su}), it ignites without transport or ground. If it is pure (\mathrm{Me}), it circulates without ignition or seal. If it is pure (\mathrm{Sa}), it binds or ends without actual transformation. Appendix G therefore imposes the triad sufficiency condition:[\operatorname{LawfulTriad}(\mathcal R)=1\iff\mathrm{TriadPlan}\text{ contains every phase required by its intent class.}]The live corpus already encodes the same structure in two ways: first by explicitly naming AppG’s Su, Me, and Sa nodes, allocator, router, and terminator; second by placing AppG on the Fire execution side of the appendix ring where recipe packets and jar macroframes are routed.

The second invariant is dosage. A lawful recipe packet must know not only what to do, but how much of each transform to apply. Let the dosage vector be

[

\mathbf d

(d_{\mathrm{mat}},d_{\mathrm{iter}},d_{\mathrm{heat}},d_{\mathrm{branch}},d_{\mathrm{risk}}),]where:

(d_{\mathrm{mat}}) is the quantity or payload mass,

(d_{\mathrm{iter}}) is the iteration count or repetition budget,

(d_{\mathrm{heat}}) is the catalytic intensity,

(d_{\mathrm{branch}}) is the maximum active branch fanout,

(d_{\mathrm{risk}}) is the corridor-bounded hazard allowance.

Then the dosage law is[\operatorname{DoseOK}(\mathcal R,c)=1\iff\forall j,\ d_j(\mathcal R)\le \beta^{(G)}_j(c),]where (\beta^{(G)}(c)) is the fire-side corridor budget inherited from Appendix F. This makes dosage exact rather than metaphorical. It is the quantitative guard against over-ignition, unbounded branching, and covert action inflation. The current corpus already treats AppG as the appendix of dosage and macroframe-bound recipe execution, so dosage must be one of its primary laws.

The third invariant is the jar frame. A recipe is not lawful if it floats free of its executable vessel. Let the jar frame be

[

\mathcal J

\big(\mathrm{FrameID},\mathrm{AllowedInputs},\mathrm{AllowedTransforms},\mathrm{HazardClass},\mathrm{Capacity},\mathrm{ExitModes}\big).]The recipe packet law is then[\operatorname{JarOK}(\mathcal R,\mathcal J)=1\iff\mathrm{Inputs}(\mathcal R)\subseteq \mathrm{AllowedInputs}(\mathcal J)\land\mathrm{Ops}(\mathcal R)\subseteq \mathrm{AllowedTransforms}(\mathcal J)\land\mathbf d(\mathcal R)\le \mathrm{Capacity}(\mathcal J).]This jar law synthesizes the current Fire-hub language of “jar macroframes” with the broader manuscript insistence that all active transformation occurs inside lawful interiors, corridors, and boundary-bounded carriers. Appendix G therefore treats jar frames as the operatoric realization of corridor-local embodiment at the execution layer.

The fourth invariant is recursion control. The corpus’s current AppG skeleton already assigns to it the explicit law

[

D_{\mathrm{rec}} \le D_{\max}.

]

Appendix G therefore fixes recursion depth not as an implementation afterthought, but as one of the defining dimensions of recipe legality. Let a recipe expansion tree be

[

\mathcal T_{\mathcal R},

]

whose nodes are subrecipes or branch packets emitted by the parent recipe. Define the recursion depth

[

D_{\mathrm{rec}}(\mathcal R)

\max{\text{depth of a node in }\mathcal T_{\mathcal R}}.]Then[\operatorname{RecOK}(\mathcal R)=1\iffD_{\mathrm{rec}}(\mathcal R)\le D_{\max}(\mathrm{Intent},c).]If this fails, the packet is illegal or must be downgraded into a bounded residual state. This law is essential because AppG is the first point where the manuscript’s intelligence becomes executable enough to risk runaway self-application. The appendix therefore serves as the first hard governor against unbounded fire.

Because AppG is a Fire appendix rather than a pure Square appendix, it must not merely store static recipes. It must also define the lawful execution braid. Let the runtime state of a recipe packet be

[

s_t=

\big(

x_t,,

\theta_t,,

\ell_t,,

d_t,,

w_t

\big),

]

where (x_t) is the current payload state, (\theta_t) is the current phase in the triad plan, (\ell_t) is the active location/jar context, (d_t) is the remaining dosage budget, and (w_t) is the active witness state. A single lawful recipe step is then

[

s_{t+1}

\operatorname{Step}_{\mathcal R}(s_t)

\big(\operatorname{Apply}{\theta_t}(x_t),,\theta{t+1},,\ell_{t+1},,d_{t+1},,w_{t+1}\big),]subject to guard constraints[\operatorname{LawfulStep}(s_t)=\mathbf 1!\Big[\operatorname{DoseOK}(s_t)\land\operatorname{JarOK}(s_t)\land\operatorname{RecOK}(s_t)\land\operatorname{GateOK}(s_t)\land\operatorname{TruthOK}(s_t)\Big].]This law is the true heart of Appendix G: it turns a recipe packet into a finite-state executable machine. The current corpus already points in this direction by giving AppG nodes for the catalyst, conductor, and ground roles and by tying it to Fire execution, recipe chemistry, and dosage macroframes. The present appendix makes those hints fully explicit.

The gate condition (\operatorname{GateOK}) links AppG back to Appendix E. No recipe step may ignite merely because the packet exists. It must be phase-permitted. Likewise, (\operatorname{TruthOK}) links AppG to Appendices D and F: an action packet cannot promote itself to execution if its corridor and constitutional truth posture do not authorize that step. In this way AppG is neither autonomous nor secondary. It is the fire hub of an already seated, law-bound, timed, and truth-disciplined organism.

A lawful recipe packet must also emit receipts. At minimum, the execution ledger must contain

[

\mathcal L_G

\big(\mathrm{RecipeID},\mathrm{StepIndex},\mathrm{TriadPhase},\mathrm{DoseSpent},\mathrm{JarFrame},\mathrm{BranchCount},\mathrm{ResidualOut},\mathrm{WitnessDelta},\mathrm{OutcomeHash}\big).]

This ledger is what keeps AppG from becoming uncontrolled dramaturgy. Every ignition, every route handoff, every seal event, every residual emission, and every recursion step must be recorded. This is structurally necessary because later appendices—especially H, I, J, and M—need to read back the exact fire-side path taken if they are to reflect, value, close, and replay it lawfully.

The appendix must also define residual and bounded incompleteness on the execution side. Not every recipe concludes in hard closure, and not every lawful packet should be forced to do so. Accordingly, AppG defines the residual output bundle

[

\mathcal R_{\mathrm{res}}

\big(\mathrm{UnspentDose},\mathrm{DeferredOps},\mathrm{BranchDebt},\mathrm{UpgradeNeed},\mathrm{ReplayNeed}\big).]If a recipe cannot terminate in a closed result within its budgets but still remains lawful, the packet may emit a bounded residual rather than fail. This links Appendix G directly to AppJ and AppL in the current metro grammar, where NEAR residuals and AMBIG evidence plans form the lawful downstream of incomplete or underdetermined execution. The body-to-appendix coupling chart in the live corpus already supports this by routing Chapters 13–15 through G, H, J, and L as “recipe/opcode chemistry, feasibility, NEAR, AMBIG.”

The appendix must further define failure. There are at least six canonical fire-side failure families.

First, overdose, where (\mathbf d) breaches corridor or jar capacity.Second, runaway recursion, where (D_{\mathrm{rec}} > D_{\max}).Third, ungrounded ignition, where Su steps fire without lawful Me or Sa continuation.Fourth, transport fracture, where the Me braid routes payload into a nonadmissible state.Fifth, premature sealing, where Sa closes a packet that still carries mandatory unresolved debt.Sixth, branch overload, where the branch count exceeds lawful fanout.

Each of these must map to a typed failure packet rather than generic error language. This requirement is forced by the truth and corridor appendices: any fire-side illegality must be expressible in a way that downstream contradiction, quarantine, or residual appendices can absorb without ambiguity.

Because the live corpus also associates AppG with triangle control, the appendix must define the exact Tria Prima legality law. Let the triadic occupancy count of a recipe packet be

[

\mathbf n(\mathcal R)

(n_{\mathrm{Su}},n_{\mathrm{Me}},n_{\mathrm{Sa}}),]where each component counts the number of active steps or weighted expenditure assigned to that role. The minimum legality condition is that (\mathbf n(\mathcal R)) lie in the intent-admissible polytope[\mathcal P_{\mathrm{intent}} \subseteq \mathbb N^3.]This allows some packets to be ignition-heavy, others routing-heavy, and others seal-heavy, while still forbidding degenerate recipes that break the declared action kind. The exact shape of (\mathcal P_{\mathrm{intent}}) is a law object from Appendix B and a corridor-constrained parameter from Appendix F, but Appendix G is where the triadic control vector is actually measured and enforced.

Appendix G must finally expose its place in the chapter orbit. The current corpus’s strongest body-to-appendix coupling places AppG beneath Chapters 10–15, precisely where manifestation, execution, jar chemistry, and benchmark fire occur. This is the correct interpretation. AppG is the fire-side execution organ of the middle arc of the manuscript. It is where the seed stops merely being described and starts doing work. That is why its relation to AppH is so important: AppG executes, but AppH checks whether the execution can lawfully compose. AppG also hands residuals to AppJ and ambiguity loads to AppL, which is why the fire hub belongs inside a larger truth and feasibility rail rather than standing alone.

In compressed form, the governing law of Appendix G may be written as[\boxed{\text{A lawful executable act in the manuscript is a typed recipe packet whose ignition, transport, and sealing phases are explicitly braided, whose dosage and recursion remain corridor-bounded, whose macroframe is declared, and whose every step emits a witness-bearing execution ledger.}}]This is why Appendix G must follow the earlier infrastructure appendices. Once the manuscript knows what a lawful object is, where it is seated, how it may be judged, and when it may be released, it must still learn how to act without burning through its own law.

Appendix G

Appendix H — Feasibility Spine, Detector Catalog, and Coupling Topology

Appendix H is the manuscript’s feasibility and defect-intelligence hub. Appendix A assigns lawful address. Appendix B states admissibility law. Appendix C gives the finite kernel and indexed transport substrate. Appendix D determines constitutional truth state. Appendix E governs release and timing cadence. Appendix F fixes corridor admissibility. Appendix G turns lawful packets into executable fire-side recipes. Appendix H decides whether those packets, transforms, routes, bridges, rewrites, and deployments are actually feasible, which defects block them, what evidence must be emitted, where the topology fractures, and which isolation or repair paths remain lawful. In the live corpus, this role appears with unusual consistency: AppH is the detector catalog and evidence-payload appendix in the transport calculus, the feasibility bridge in the recipe and fire-arc materials, and the coupling/topology appendix responsible for dependency closure, cut-sets, and isolation partitions in the outer crystal map. These are not separate jobs. They are one coherent function: Appendix H is the place where the crystal becomes aware of its own breakpoints and admissible continuations.

The governing thesis of Appendix H is therefore:

[\boxed{\text{No route, transform, recipe, repair, publication, or tunnel is lawful unless the active coupling topology is explicit, all relevant detectors have either passed or emitted typed evidence, and a feasibility bridge certifies that the intended crossing preserves corridor legality under the current cut-set structure.}}]

This thesis places Appendix H at the exact midpoint between possibility and execution. A candidate may be well-addressed, well-lawed, well-typed, well-timed, and still infeasible because its dependencies do not close, its environment has fractured, its safety gates are blocked, its numerical drift is too large, its branch fanout is unsafe, or its intended bridge crosses a forbidden partition. Appendix H is where these distinctions become formal. The live corpus already makes this explicit by routing all repair/control traffic through AppH, by designating ST-DETECT as ((\mathrm{Ch09}+\mathrm{AppH})), and by identifying AppH as the feasibility bridge between recipe packets and executable chemistry.

The primitive object of the appendix is the feasibility packet. A feasibility packet is not a truth packet and not a recipe packet; it is the packet that decides whether a candidate move can proceed, under what class of feasibility, and what typed defect evidence must be retained if it cannot. The canonical form is

[

\Phi

\big(\mathrm{FeatID},\mathrm{Target},\mathrm{TopoCtx},\mathrm{DetectorSet},\mathrm{EvidenceSet},\mathrm{CutSet},\mathrm{IsoClass},\mathrm{BridgeClass},\mathrm{Verdict},\mathrm{RepairHooks},\omega_\Phi\big),]

where (\mathrm{FeatID}) is the packet identity, (\mathrm{Target}) is the route, operator, recipe, or publication act being tested, (\mathrm{TopoCtx}) is the active coupling topology context, (\mathrm{DetectorSet}) is the set of required detectors, (\mathrm{EvidenceSet}) is the payload family emitted by those detectors, (\mathrm{CutSet}) is the active cut-set witness, (\mathrm{IsoClass}) is the current isolation regime, (\mathrm{BridgeClass}) is the admissible crossing family, (\mathrm{Verdict}\in{\mathrm{Exact},\mathrm{Bounded},\mathrm{Probe},\mathrm{Blocked}}), (\mathrm{RepairHooks}) is the set of lawful next actions, and (\omega_\Phi) is the witness bundle certifying that the feasibility judgment itself is lawful. This form synthesizes the live corpus’s three strongest signals about AppH: detector registry and evidence payloads, feasibility bridge semantics, and coupling/cut-set topology.

The first invariant of Appendix H is topological explicitness. A feasibility judgment may not be issued against a hidden dependency field. Every target (x) must be evaluated inside a coupling graph

[

\mathcal G_H(x)

(V_x,E_x,\tau_x,\chi_x),]

where (V_x) is the required node set, (E_x) is the required dependency or interaction edge set, (\tau_x) is the edge typing family, and (\chi_x) is the corridor and trust decoration family needed to interpret the graph. The graph may live at different scales—packet neighborhood, operator dependency closure, runtime subsystem boundary, publication pipeline—but it must always be explicit. This is exactly the coupling/topology role assigned to AppH in the outer-crystal map: dependency closure, cut-sets, and isolation partitions are not auxiliary diagrams; they are first-class legal substrates.

The second invariant is detector completeness. Let the global detector family of the appendix be

[

\mathfrak D_H

{d_{\mathrm{schema}},d_{\mathrm{scope}},d_{\mathrm{budget}},d_{\mathrm{drift}},d_{\mathrm{contr}},d_{\mathrm{witness}},d_{\mathrm{replay}},d_{\mathrm{topo}},d_{\mathrm{bridge}},d_{\mathrm{safety}}}.]

Each detector (d_i) is a deterministic predicate with a typed evidence payload. The exact minimum meanings are as follows. (d_{\mathrm{schema}}) checks structural and schema consistency; (d_{\mathrm{scope}}) checks target legality under corridor scope; (d_{\mathrm{budget}}) checks resource feasibility; (d_{\mathrm{drift}}) checks that declared bounds are not exceeded; (d_{\mathrm{contr}}) checks conflict and contradiction constraints; (d_{\mathrm{witness}}) checks proof-bearing sufficiency and anti-laundering; (d_{\mathrm{replay}}) checks replay closure; (d_{\mathrm{topo}}) checks graph connectivity, dependency reachability, and cut-set stability; (d_{\mathrm{bridge}}) checks that any intended crossing or transport is legal between the active local domains; and (d_{\mathrm{safety}}) checks corridor hard invariants that block execution or publication. The transport calculus corpus already names AppH as the detector catalog and evidence payload appendix, and later routes repeatedly pass through AppH when drift, repair, or control are at issue. Appendix H therefore freezes the detector family as a constitutional registry rather than a loose utilities module.

A detector must emit typed evidence. The canonical detector evidence object is

[

\mathcal E

\big(\mathrm{DetID},\mathrm{TargetDigest},\mathrm{ContextDigest},\mathrm{Status},\mathrm{Code},\mathrm{Measure},\mathrm{Threshold},\mathrm{Refs},\omega_{\mathcal E}\big),]

where (\mathrm{Status}\in{\mathrm{PASS},\mathrm{NEAR},\mathrm{FAIL}}), (\mathrm{Code}) is the specific defect code, (\mathrm{Measure}) is the measured violation or safety signal, (\mathrm{Threshold}) is the governing corridor bound, and (\mathrm{Refs}) are the referenced objects, traces, witnesses, or receipts that made the judgment reproducible. This aligns directly with the transport corpus, which already treats AppH as the home of defect taxonomy, evidence payloads, and trigger mapping, and with the corridor chapter, which requires earliest-failure localization and typed evidence objects for hard and near failures.

The appendix’s third invariant is cut-set legality. A feasibility problem is often not “can this step run?” but “what exactly must remain connected for this step to run without semantic or operational fracture?” Accordingly, Appendix H defines the cut-set object

[

\mathcal K_H

\big(\mathrm{Nodes},\mathrm{Edges},\mathrm{BoundaryClass},\mathrm{BridgeNeed},\mathrm{FailureIfCut}\big).]

A cut-set is not merely the graph-theoretic set whose removal disconnects a graph. It is the legally minimal set of nodes and edges whose integrity is required for the target action to remain admissible. The topological feasibility predicate therefore takes the form

[\operatorname{TopoOK}(x)=1\iff\forall K\in \operatorname{ReqCut}(x),\K\subseteq \mathcal G_H(x)\land\operatorname{Closed}(K)=1.]

This is the strict topological reading of the corpus’s phrase “cut-sets and isolation partitions.” AppH is not only about finding defects after the fact. It is about knowing in advance which topological support is minimally necessary.

The fourth invariant is isolation. When topological or detector failure occurs, the system must be able to contain the failing region without lying about its state. Thus Appendix H defines an isolation partition family

[

\mathcal I_H

{\mathrm{LocalIso},\mathrm{SubsystemIso},\mathrm{RouteIso},\mathrm{QuarantineIso},\mathrm{ProbeIso}}.]

A target is assigned an isolation class when detectors or topology indicate that normal composition would be unsafe or misleading. In transport language, this blocks unsafe propagation. In fire-side execution language, this prevents unfeasible recipe chemistry from becoming deployment. In publication language, this blocks export of unresolved or miscoupled states. The current corpus already shows this exact role by routing repair/control and governance lines through AppH and by requiring evidence payloads and isolation behavior before downstream release.

The fifth invariant is bridge typing. Appendix H is not only a detector appendix; it is also the place where the system decides whether a crossing between two local regimes is exact, bounded, probe-feasible, or blocked. Accordingly, the bridge family is

[

\mathfrak B_H

{\mathsf{ExactBridge},\mathsf{BoundedBridge},\mathsf{ProbeBridge},\mathsf{BlockedBridge}}.]

An exact bridge is one whose transport preserves the declared invariant bundle exactly and within corridor budgets. A bounded bridge is one whose transport remains within an explicit defect or drift envelope and therefore may support NEAR but not unconditional OK claims. A probe bridge is one whose crossing may be attempted only as a bounded exploration, usually under ambiguity or partial evidence. A blocked bridge is forbidden under current law, topology, or safety regime. This classification synthesizes the corpus’s transport law, AppH feasibility role, and the recipe-execution materials that distinguish exact feasibility, bounded feasibility, infeasibility, and probe-feasibility.

The canonical feasibility verdict of Appendix H is therefore not the same as the truth spine of Appendix D. The verdict set is

[

\mathbb F_H

{\mathrm{Exact},\mathrm{Bounded},\mathrm{Probe},\mathrm{Blocked}}.]

Its semantics are:

Exact: all required detectors pass, topology closes, and the required bridge class is exact.

Bounded: the target remains lawful under an explicit residual envelope and may require downgrade downstream.

Probe: the target may only be explored as a bounded test action and may not be promoted to commitment or publication without further evidence.

Blocked: one or more hard detectors or cut-set obligations fail decisively.

This distinction is necessary because truth and feasibility are not identical. A theorem may be true but operationally infeasible to execute under current budgets. A route may be topologically legal but corridor-blocked. A recipe may be structurally available but only probe-feasible. Appendix H is where those differences become typed.

Because AppH sits in the FacetBase(3) role in the outer-crystal map, it must also support construction and decomposition operations. Thus the appendix must define a coupling closure operator[\operatorname{Close}_H(\mathcal G)]that computes the minimal lawful closure of the dependency graph required for a target, and a cut-set extractor[\operatorname{Cut}_H(\mathcal G,x)]that returns the minimal legal cut-sets for the target (x). The exactness of these operators matters because later repair and optimization chapters depend on knowing whether a missing bridge, missing witness edge, or missing replay dependency is the first lawful bottleneck. The transport calculus corpus is explicit that repair/control begins with defect evidence and trigger mapping from AppH; Appendix H makes that computable.

The appendix must also bind directly to Chapter 10’s certified repair machinery. The repair route given in the corpus is

[

\mathrm{Ch09} \to \mathrm{AppH} \to \mathrm{Ch10} \to \mathrm{Ch13} \to \mathrm{Ch14},

]

which means the detector layer is the lawful upstream of repair, proof regeneration, and receipt closure. Appendix H therefore must emit not only defect evidence but repair hooks. A repair hook is a typed next-step pointer

[

\mathcal R_H

\big(\mathrm{DefectCode},\mathrm{RepairClass},\mathrm{PrereqSet},\mathrm{EscalationNeed},\mathrm{ExpectedBridge}\big).]This object is what allows Chapter 10’s repair program to compile deterministically from detected fracture rather than by free search. The corpus already implies this when it speaks of trigger mapping and repair/control lines passing through AppH.

The appendix must further normalize defect codes. The defect taxonomy should minimally cover:

Schema defects — object or packet fails its own declared form.

Convention defects — input/output spec mismatch, missing bridge, or implicit coercion.

Witness defects — binding mismatch, insufficient level, missing local-check data.

Replay defects — nondeterministic rerun, hash divergence, missing dependency.

Budget defects — compute/write/branch/tolerance excess.

Topology defects — disconnected dependency closure, missing node or edge, illegal cycle, violated cut-set.

Safety defects — route or action crosses a prohibited corridor.

Execution defects — recipe or operator step is infeasible or underdetermined under current conditions.

Publication defects — attempted export without adequate replay or contradiction clearance.

Each code must map to a detector, an evidence schema, an isolation class, and a repair hook. This ensures the appendix can function not only as a diagnostic list, but as a deterministic defect-to-action machine.

Because the manuscript is generator-first, Appendix H must not store defects as undifferentiated prose. It must store the detector registry[\mathfrak D_H=(\mathcal D,\mathcal E,\mathcal R,\mathcal I,\mathfrak B_H)]compactly enough that specific detector instances can be generated as needed from the target type, active corridor, and route class. In practice, this means every detector entry should be decomposed into:

target domain,

preconditions,

measured predicate,

severity mapping,

evidence payload schema,

repair hook schema,

legal downstream routes.

This is the proper “catalog” role already assigned to AppH in the corpus.

The final and deepest role of Appendix H is to prevent the crystal from lying about its own continuity. A system without AppH can still route, witness, replay, and even publish, but it cannot say with rigor when those acts are topologically fragile, budget-fractured, or only boundedly feasible. AppH is therefore the appendix of honest coupling. It says where the system really holds, where it only nearly holds, where it may be probed, and where it must stop. That is why it belongs exactly where the corpus places it: between execution, truth, repair, governance, and publication.

In compressed form, the governing law of Appendix H may be written as[\boxed{\text{A manuscript act is feasible if and only if the active coupling topology closes, the required detector family passes or emits bounded admissible evidence, the relevant cut-sets remain intact, and any crossing between local regimes is certified by an exact, bounded, or probe bridge rather than guessed by continuity.}}]This is why Appendix H is indispensable. It is the place where the crystal learns the shape of its own possibility.

Appendix H

Appendix I — Truth Corridors, Verdict Frames, and the Σ-Truth Spine

Appendix I is the mandatory truth hub of the appendix crystal. It is not the place where objects are addressed, because that belongs to Appendix A. It is not the place where replay closure is finalized, because that belongs to Appendix M. It is the place where an addressed object, route, packet, operator, release candidate, or runtime state is assigned its lawful truth corridor, its admissibility bounds, and its active verdict frame under a non-null context. In the live corpus, AppI is repeatedly fixed as the truth corridor appendix, the cloud-side lens base, and the middle node of the non-bypass support skeleton[\Sigma={\mathrm{AppA},\mathrm{AppI},\mathrm{AppM}},]which means no strong route is lawful unless it passes through Entry, Truth, and Replay in that order.

The governing thesis of the appendix is therefore:

[\boxed{\text{A manuscript state is not lawfully true merely because it has support; it is lawfully true only when its active corridor, admissibility budget, context pointer, and verdict frame are fixed, replay-linked, and stable for the current tick.}}]

This thesis gives Appendix I a distinct constitutional role even in the presence of other truth-bearing appendices. Appendix D carries constitutional proof packets and the exact obligations attached to a truth state. Appendix F carries corridor algebra, requiredness maps, and admissibility policy. Appendix I is the active runtime hub where those laws are instantiated into a verdict frame on a concrete target and then routed onward toward residual work, ambiguity work, quarantine, replay closure, or publication. The corpus makes this exact role visible from multiple angles: AppI is named as “Truth Corridors,” “truth overlays / corridor classes / verdict frames / Σ-truth spine,” and “mandatory truth hub / corridor classification / admissibility budgets / abstain law.”

The primitive object of Appendix I is the truth-corridor frame. Its canonical form is

[

\Xi

\big(\tau,,C_\tau,,B_{\mathrm{adm}},,\chi,,\mathcal W,,\mathcal R,,\mathcal O,,\omega_\Xi\big),]

where (\tau\in{\mathrm{OK},\mathrm{NEAR},\mathrm{AMBIG},\mathrm{FAIL}}) is the truth state, (C_\tau) is the active corridor, (B_{\mathrm{adm}}) is the admissibility budget vector, (\chi) is the non-null context pointer, (\mathcal W) is the active witness family, (\mathcal R) is the replay requirement family, (\mathcal O) is the downstream obligation or overlay family, and (\omega_\Xi) is the witness certifying that the frame itself was lawfully assigned. This exact object is already latent in the live corpus’s 4×4 appendix crystal skeleton, where AppI’s Square-side schema is given as truth state (\tau), corridor (C_\tau), bounds (B_{\mathrm{adm}}), and context pointer, and its constructive side is given as state assigner, corridor opener, bounds evaluator, and context binder.

The first invariant of Appendix I is the truth lattice itself. Let

[

\mathbb T

{\mathrm{OK},\mathrm{NEAR},\mathrm{AMBIG},\mathrm{FAIL}}.]

These are not rhetorical confidence levels. They are the only lawful verdict classes AppI may emit. (\mathrm{OK}) means that the target is corridor-legal, burden-complete for the active scope, and replay-admissible at the promised level. (\mathrm{NEAR}) means that the target remains lawful but still carries bounded residual obligations. (\mathrm{AMBIG}) means that multiple candidates remain admissible and no lawful dominance principle yet collapses them. (\mathrm{FAIL}) means contradiction, illegality, or corridor break is decisive. The live corpus fixes this exact quartet in AppI’s law skeleton and repeatedly treats it as the truth classification layer of the entire crystal.

The second invariant is the abstention law. Appendix I adopts as hard constitutional principle

[\boxed{\mathrm{ABSTAIN}>\mathrm{GUESS}.}]

This is not a stylistic preference. It is the corridor discipline that prevents the crystal from forcing premature closure. If the active corridor does not support (\mathrm{OK}), then (\mathrm{OK}) is not available. If it does not support lawful narrowing from candidate multiplicity, then (\mathrm{AMBIG}) must remain visible. If the support is bounded but incomplete, the correct result is (\mathrm{NEAR}) with explicit residuals. The corpus is explicit on this point in both the appendix ring and the chapter-body abstract: truth is corridor-typed, and abstention outranks guessing.

The third invariant is context non-nullity. A truth assignment is illegal unless its context pointer exists and is fixed. Formally,

[\chi \neq \varnothing.]

The context pointer (\chi) binds the target to the active corridor, route signature, scope, ledger epoch, and sometimes the current chapter or appendix rail posture. This is why the corpus includes “Context Pointer” as one of the four primitive objects of AppI and separately insists that no strong route is lawful without AppA, AppI, and AppM all present in the hub sequence. The context pointer is what stops truth from being evaluated in a vacuum; the Σ-spine is what stops it from being evaluated outside address and replay.

The fourth invariant is per-tick immutability. Once a target has been assigned a truth frame at a fixed scheduler tick, the verdict may not silently mutate within that same tick. If it changes, the change must be represented as a new corridor event with a new witness and a new route ledger entry. The live corpus already states this as an AppI law atom: “immutability per tick.” Appendix I therefore adopts the formal law

[\operatorname{Tick}(x)=t\Longrightarrow\forall t' = t,\\operatorname{Verdict}(x,t')=\operatorname{Verdict}(x,t)]unless an explicit state-transition receipt has been emitted. This makes temporal truth hygiene exact and prevents covert verdict drift inside one evaluation cycle.

The active corridor (C_\tau) is the fifth invariant. A corridor is the admissibility region in which a verdict is allowed to count. It binds the target to requiredness class, trust and drift bounds, route family, and publication rights. Appendix F supplies the general corridor algebra; Appendix I instantiates it at the verdict layer. Thus AppI’s corridor law is not[\tau \Rightarrow C_\tau]as mere naming, but rather[\operatorname{Allow}{C}(x)\subseteq \mathbb T,\qquad\tau\in \operatorname{Allow}{C}(x).]Only truth states admitted by the active corridor may be assigned. This is why the corpus repeatedly couples AppI to Chapters 10–12, 16–18, and the deepest appendix rails: AppI is the place where truth moves from a possible classification to an allowed one.

The admissibility budget (B_{\mathrm{adm}}) is the sixth invariant. AppI does not invent all budget dimensions—that work is distributed across Appendices B, C, F, H, and J—but it is where the current corridor’s effective bounds are tested against the target. The minimum schema is

[

B_{\mathrm{adm}}

\big(

b_{\mathrm{witness}},

b_{\mathrm{replay}},

b_{\mathrm{drift}},

b_{\mathrm{route}},

b_{\mathrm{scope}}

\big).

]

A candidate state may be promoted only if these bounds are satisfied in the active corridor:

[

\operatorname{BoundsOK}(x,C_\tau)

\mathbf 1!\Big[\mu_{\mathrm{witness}}\le b_{\mathrm{witness}}\land\mu_{\mathrm{replay}}\le b_{\mathrm{replay}}\land\mu_{\mathrm{drift}}\le b_{\mathrm{drift}}\land\mu_{\mathrm{route}}\le b_{\mathrm{route}}\land\mu_{\mathrm{scope}}\le b_{\mathrm{scope}}\Big].]This law is already visible in the live corpus through AppI’s explicit “Bounds (B_{\mathrm{adm}}),” “Bounds Evaluator,” and “Bounds Check Proof” stations.

From these invariants, Appendix I defines its core constructive operators. The first is the state assigner[\operatorname{Assign}I(x,\chi)\to \tau,]which computes the candidate truth class before promotion discipline and overlay routing. The second is the corridor opener[\operatorname{Open}{I}(x,\chi)\to C_\tau,]which determines the active corridor instance under which the candidate may be judged. The third is the bounds evaluator[\operatorname{Eval}{I}(x,C\tau)\to B_{\mathrm{state}},]which returns the actual measured burden state against the admissibility budget. The fourth is the context binder[\operatorname{Bind}_{I}(x,\chi)\to \Xi,]which packages all of the above into the truth-corridor frame. These four operators are not inferred from the chapter system; they are explicitly required by the live AppI skeleton.

The state assignment law itself is tiered. Let the raw evaluation predicates be:[\mathrm{Contradiction}(x),\quad\mathrm{CandidateMultiplicity}(x),\quad\mathrm{ResidualBounded}(x),\quad\mathrm{ClosureComplete}(x).]Then the canonical assignment is:[\operatorname{Assign}I(x,\chi)=\begin{cases}\mathrm{FAIL}, & \mathrm{Contradiction}(x)=1,\\mathrm{AMBIG}, & \mathrm{Contradiction}(x)=0 \land \mathrm{CandidateMultiplicity}(x)>1,\\mathrm{NEAR}, & \mathrm{Contradiction}(x)=0 \land \mathrm{CandidateMultiplicity}(x)=1 \land \mathrm{ResidualBounded}(x)=1 \land \mathrm{ClosureComplete}(x)=0,\\mathrm{OK}, & \mathrm{ClosureComplete}(x)=1 \land \operatorname{BoundsOK}(x,C\tau)=1.\end{cases}]This hierarchy is consistent with the live corpus’s routing laws and with the chapter-side abstract that repeatedly uses the same four-valued truth discipline. Appendix I’s distinct contribution is that it binds that hierarchy to corridor and budget, rather than leaving it as a floating truth taxonomy.

Appendix I must also define verdict overlays. Once (\tau) is set, the appendix determines the lawful downstream truth rail:[\operatorname{TruthOverlay}(\tau,\mathrm{Intent})=\begin{cases}\varnothing, & \tau=\mathrm{OK}\land \mathrm{Intent}\neq \mathrm{PUBLISH},\{\mathrm{AppO}}, & \tau=\mathrm{OK}\land \mathrm{Intent}=\mathrm{PUBLISH},\{\mathrm{AppJ}}, & \tau=\mathrm{NEAR},\{\mathrm{AppL}}, & \tau=\mathrm{AMBIG},\{\mathrm{AppK}}, & \tau=\mathrm{FAIL}.\end{cases}]This routing law is one of the deepest pieces of evidence for Appendix I’s canonical role, because the live corpus repeats it across the deterministic router, the appendix-only metro map, and the truth descent rail. AppI is the hinge where unresolvedness becomes lawfully metabolized into residuals, ambiguity workbench, contradiction/quarantine, replay closure, and possibly public release.

Because AppI sits on the Σ-spine, it must also expose a canonical digest. The minimum certificate family is:

[

\mathcal H_I

\big(\mathrm{TruthHash},\mathrm{CorridorActiveLog},\mathrm{BoundsCheckProof},\mathrm{CtxValidCert}\big).]The TruthHash is the stable digest of ((x,\tau,C_\tau,\chi)). The CorridorActiveLog records which corridor instance was actually open at assignment time. The BoundsCheckProof certifies the admissibility evaluation. The CtxValidCert proves that the context pointer was non-null and schema-valid. This exactly matches the live appendix skeleton’s fourth facet for AppI.

The appendix must also formalize downgrade law. A truth verdict is not permanent merely because it was once assigned. If the corridor narrows, drift grows, support decays, or contradiction emerges, the verdict must be downgraded lawfully. Let[\operatorname{Down}_I:\mathbb T\times \mathcal E \to \mathbb T]be the downgrade operator. Its minimum legal transitions are:[\mathrm{OK}\to \mathrm{NEAR},\ \mathrm{AMBIG},\ \mathrm{FAIL},\qquad\mathrm{NEAR}\to \mathrm{AMBIG},\ \mathrm{FAIL},\qquad\mathrm{AMBIG}\to \mathrm{FAIL}]when the relevant evidence (\mathcal E) justifies it. No silent downgrade is allowed. Every downgrade must emit a new truth-corridor frame and a new certificate family. This law is required because the corpus repeatedly treats AppI as the live corridor and verdict hub rather than a one-shot truth label store.

The appendix must further forbid four classes of error. First, truth without corridor: no verdict may be issued if (\chi=\varnothing) or (C_\tau) is unspecified. Second, guess instead of abstain: no forced (\mathrm{OK}) or (\mathrm{NEAR}) may replace a lawful (\mathrm{AMBIG}). Third, budget laundering: a candidate may not be promoted by silently widening (B_{\mathrm{adm}}). Fourth, tick drift: an assigned verdict may not mutate inside one clock interval without an explicit transition receipt. These prohibitions are already latent in the live AppI law atoms and the routing corpus’s insistence on a non-bypass Entry/Truth/Replay spine.

The final and deepest role of Appendix I is to make truth a live corridor phenomenon rather than a static label. A claim is not “true” in the abstract. It is true, nearly true, ambiguous, or failed under a corridor, under a context, under budgets, and under a tick. This is why AppI is simultaneously part of the lens cycle[\mathrm{AppC}\leftrightarrow\mathrm{AppE}\leftrightarrow\mathrm{AppI}\leftrightarrow\mathrm{AppM}\leftrightarrow\mathrm{AppC}]and part of the non-bypass Σ-spine[\mathrm{AppA}\leftrightarrow\mathrm{AppI}\leftrightarrow\mathrm{AppM}.]It is both the Cloud-side evaluator and the mandatory truth gate. Without it, the crystal could route and replay, but it would not know under what conditions a route or replay counted as truth. The live corpus presents exactly this dual role, and Appendix I formalizes it as manuscript law.

In compressed form, the governing law of Appendix I may be written as[\boxed{\text{A manuscript verdict is lawful if and only if an addressed target is bound to a non-null context, placed in an explicit truth corridor, evaluated against declared admissibility budgets, assigned one of the four truth states without guessing, and routed through the Σ-truth spine with replay-visible certificates.}}]This is why Appendix I is indispensable. It is the place where the manuscript stops merely carrying claims and begins carrying legally typed truth.

Appendix I

Appendix K — Quarantined Void, Failure Packets, and Reentry Law

Appendix K is the manuscript’s fail sink, quarantine chamber, and recovery threshold. In the active corpus, the legacy appendix field names Appendix K explicitly as Quarantined Void, while the live runtime and connective-tissue materials route every decisive FAIL state to AppK rather than to publication, bounded work, or ambiguity handling. The same branch makes this routing structural rather than optional: the default truth overlay sends FAIL to AppK, multiple named circuits terminate their fail paths there, and the larger organism is repeatedly described as requiring quarantine, repair, replay, and only then reentry, reseed, or revocation. Appendix K is therefore not a miscellaneous failure appendix.
